		<shortDescription>See it in a theater and depart Awake!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"Inland Empire" introduces a new character to Lynchian cinema--the audience. I'm surprised that nobody has mentioned this elephant in the living room--especially since it's so obvious in the rabbit's living room. "A woman in trouble" is an archetype. We came to see a woman in trouble and, clever wag that he is, David Lynch has shown us ourselves watching a woman in trouble.After "Mulholland Drive", the astute viewer is on the lookout to ask--"Who is the dreamer?" David Lynch suggests that this movie is about transcendental meditation and that we approach the whole film as a dream. We are the dreamer, even if we are led through a Lynchian artistic representation of our dream. As may be expected, it does not resonate with everybody's unconscious--sorry. We (the unseen seer) appear early, the elephant in the rabbit's living room. Who's trip down the rabbit hole is it--not Nikki's--ours! We are he viewer towards whom the characters in the Polish apartment turn their chairs.At the end Nikki "awakes" to be in the movie, but in the theater (in the audience) she sees herself on the screen and is back in a dream. At the end, the cast speaks directly to us-"It's OK! It was only a movie! We hope you had fun!"-Then the lights come on and there we are in the audience. The movie hasn't ended; we must awake and be enlightened.Stephen R. Cann</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>How to jump down the rabbit hole &amp; land in one piece</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Inland Empire is an incredibly simple movie told in an abstract manner. The core plot could be written easily on a dinner napkin, so it is obviously not what makes this movie special. David Lynch's ability to tell the story in a free-associative, non-literal style is what makes this film one that people will be talking about for a long time.Lynch gives the audience a number of cues to show that what's on the screen is not to be taken literally -- the rabbits, for instance. Movies like Lost Highway and Mulholland Dr. toy with ideas like dreams and identity, but Inland Empire manages to turn these ideas into an entire self-contained filmic language. This is what people mean when they say it's even stranger or more experimental than Eraserhead.Please please please, do not try to analyse, delineate, and piece together every aspect of this film. You might've been able to do that with Lynch's earlier works, but with Inland Empire he is making it sharply clear that he wants you to feel out the movie, not think it through. Trying to "figure out" how character X relates to character Y and whether or not they were dreaming or not will quickly turn you into one of those people madly typing out a "Why did you fail me, David?" screed on some internet message board. If you do not learn to listen in Lynch's language, you will not understand him, guaranteed.This film requires some faith on viewers' parts. It requires you to watch films in a new way, and really be open to identifying with people. If you're not that type, and you were looking for another film full of midgets, Angelo Badalamenti, subtle lighting, etc...please go back to watching your copy of Mulholland Dr. But if you are game, Inland Empire is a treat unlike anything you've seen yet. My new Lynch favorite, 10/10</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>The New Lynch...well worth the wait.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>As an avid Lynch follower who has seen all of his works and read just about all their is on the man, I must say INLAND EMPIRE is by far his most experimental. His short films are worthy of competition in this category, but I must admit that this film has pushed Lynch further into, well further into "Lynch world". The Digital Video format has allowed Lynch to break boundaries set for him by the conventional film format and explore the truly dark and mysterious corners of the imagination. Although I found it difficult at first to decipher, it later on became one of his more easily reachable stories. I feel the 2 and a half years he spent filming INLAND EMPIRE while ignoring the typical script treatment, Lynch was able to 'feel' what the film was, build the characters based on what was being filmed and not written down in words, and find the connections through the visual/audio elements rather than force together the elements because that's what he originally intended. Even though I still believe "Mulholland Dr". to be his 'best work', INLAND EMPIRE truly impressed me and I would rank it at a close second on his all time list. This is definitely his darkest work and it will surprise most people for its wit, its nail biting moments, and its ability to throw you where you've never been before. I RECOMMEND THIS FILM to anyone who is looking for something new, and that will inspire them. But mainly because it will leave you with a mental hangover that will continue well into the next day.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>His MASTERPIECE.. A work of sheer brilliance</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>So, I've been ruminating on Inland Empire, the New David Lynch film, and I've come to the conclusion that it's a work of sheer brilliance, and his masterpiece... When I originally saw it, I tried to dissect it and come up with the 'meaning' behind it, and kind of figure out the structure and how the separate threads worked with each other.. It struck me last night, Lynch has finally accomplished something that I think he's been working towards his entire career.. He's actually filmed a dream.. Parts of other films of his have been 'dream-like' and with Mulholland drive part of the movie was actually supposed to have been a dream, but it was a film dream, meaning, it was still merely 'dream like'... This thing is actually a dream.. It hit me like a brick... This film can't be interpreted in the same way you'd interpret a regular film, this film has to be interpreted as you would interpret a dream... Lynch himself I believe is cryptically letting us in on this.. The tagline to the film is 'a woman in trouble'.. I believe the woman in trouble is the woman who's HAVING the dream.. SHe's troubled, and the nightmare she's having reflects it.. It's filled with guilt, and persecution, violence... In the interpretation of the dream, we can somehow figure out who the woman is that's having it... This is not to say that there isn't some sort of 'plot' running through the thing, however, it's not a 'movie plot' it's the kind of plot one has in a dream, it's messy and doesn't really make sense when you're awake... Even the name 'Inland Empire' suggests the inner world, the dream world (yes I know that it also refers to the LA suburb).. I'm going again this weekend, this time I will dispense with the prosaic analysis and just 'experience it' ... This is what lynch says about it, he says, 'I hope you have a good experience', he's basically letting us know, that this needs to be taken in on a different level... The film is incredibly brilliant in the pacing and editing, it starts off slowly with just a general sense of unease and 'non-rightness' that all dreams that turn darker start off like.. Then it becomes more out of control, and all of a sudden you realize it's a nightmare, and the ending.. the ending is mind blowing.. I've had dreams like this before, David Lynch has created a masterpiece, end of story.. If anyone cares about true art, they should experience this film..</fullReview>

		<username>redones</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Inland Empire - Inside Lynch's head</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I should start off by saying I am a huge David Lynch fan. The intricate complexities of Mulholland Drive and the near perfection of Blue Velvet list among some of the masterpieces of modern cinema. And I don't use the term lightly. In fact 'masterpiece' is a grossly overused word when it comes to cinema. There's practically a new 'masterpiece' released every week, judging by critic's responses. Inland Empire is not a masterpiece. It's good, but it's not a masterpiece. In terms of the plot (and I use the term 'plot' very, very loosely) it follows the rise of a once great actor (played by Laura Dern) to fame through her being cast in a new melodrama. That's about as comprehensible as it gets. For the remaining 2 hours and 25 minutes we are presented with an unintelligible mess of Lynchian madness as we see Dern's character slowly lose grip of her sanity and slip into darkness as she questions what is real and what is a product of her madness. And, ultimately, we will do the same. Dern seems to spend the whole 3 hours looking completely terrified and bewildered, but nevertheless she carries the role with a truly operatic brilliance. It's true that the film is overly long. After the first 90 minutes I began to grow tired of the brain-teasing and longed for something with clear narrative rigour. Mulholland drive is an example of clear cut linear storytelling in comparison to Inland Empire. However none of this matters because if you stick with it you will be rewarded with some real cinematic gems. It's always a treat to revisit the world of David Lynch and it really doesn't matter that nothing makes sense. If you accept that this is a film of moods and don't spend the whole 3 hours asking 'what are the people in rabbit suits? , why are they all dancing? , what the hell's the bit with the monkey and the one legged woman?' (I'm not making this up) and completely immerse yourself in the world that Lynch creates, you will enjoy, or at least appreciate, the film. To summarise, Inland Empire is a tour inside the head of David Lynch which places the greatest emphasis on the oppressive atmosphere and building sense of indefinable dread. Yes, it's overly long and would've worked better as a 90 minute snapshot of a descent into madness, but Lynch has certainly cornered the market in terms of mind-boggling surrealism. I will say this though: if you haven't seen any of Lynch's work, I advise you to watch Inland Empire last. It's so surreal and nightmarish that you will either dismiss it as obscurity for the sake of obscurity and outright refuse to watch any Lynch stuff again, or accept the dream-like logic for what it is. The film's a mess?but I think it's meant to be.</fullReview>

		<date>2013-01-25</date>
		<username>chrismaunder</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Inland Empire Review</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>David Lynch, the artist who became a director, has once again used the canvas of film to paint the violent inner colours of the human condition. In Mulholland Drive, how much better we understood Diane's mental state through her dreaming than if we had seen her in real time moping around her flat! Inland Empire is trickier to follow as the boundary between 'reality' and 'fantasy' is less clear. But Lynch does have a 'real story' in mind somewhere, although he ? returning to the pattern of Blue Velvet and Lost Highway ? is inviting you to create it for yourself. Creativity is more important than the 'right answer'. The story line is characteristically conveyed through non-sequential narrative. In this film, we never know who is experiencing the emotions we see. A woman has undergone a traumatic series of events, not necessarily outside the experience of ordinary people. What Lynch succeeds in doing is showing us what impact these events may have on the inner world of subjectivity, on the 'inland empire'.Here is my reconstructed jigsaw picture. The character whose emotions are dramatised (let us call her X) has been married. Through an extramarital affair with a man who already had a wife and family, she became pregnant. She tried to hide the infidelity by pretending that the baby was her husband's, only to find that he kept hidden from her the fact that he was infertile ('I have a secret'). As a consequence, he becomes very angry, leaves her and starts a new life with someone else. We know little about him except (perhaps!) that he has 'a way with animals'. When X turns instead to her lover and father of the child, he too rejects her. At a later point, the child ? a son ? dies. She is left with nothing but the wreckage of two relationships.We see the extent to which X's self-loathing has reached. In reflecting on her own circumstances and looking back at the events, she fantasises, using narrative lines which are perhaps available in drama that she knows ? radio and film ? but on which she superimposes her own story. Through this we see the potential of dramatic narrative to help people express powerful emotions which arise in their own apparently unglamourous lives.First, X sees herself as the woman who had everything but who lost it through circumstances that seemed at the time to be out of her control. In her recreation, Laura Dern's 'Nicky' plays that part. As an actress, Nicky begins the film in a luxurious house but, in her character as 'Susan', gradually moves down the social ladder and ends up as a prostitute with a horrendous past. Her naivety ('who could have known?') at what is about to unfold ? and she is warned about this by her 'new neighbour' - reflects X's own recollection of her lack of awareness of the 'consequences' of her 'actions'.Secondly, X uses a drama about shady Polish characters to castigate herself. Her actions have shown her to be no better than a prostitute. What do they do? They 'f***', hence the corollary: she f***s, so she must be a prostitute. A prostitute's life is lonely, stuck in a hotel room that she doesn't recognise as her own. X too is alienated from her past, her hopes and those she loved. The Polish woman alone in her room represents X in the present, looking back on the events. The dramas intertwine, as the two parts of the woman's self-characterisation ? naive, romantically stimulated Susan and the Polish prostitute ? come together. Susan finds herself to be the prostitute.X's hatred ? towards her ex-husband; his new lover; her ex-lover's wife; but mostly turned against herself ? is expressed through the idea of a 'brutal f***ing murder'. The events that inspire this metaphor have occurred in the past (as conveyed by the lapse of time in the Polish part of the story), but Susan has to live through them; by looking back, she makes them present. Only by re-experiencing her feelings in the most dramatic way can X begin to move on from them. The anguish of Susan when she is stabbed is a climactic and very powerful moment in the film. The agony of emotional pain, regret and self-condemnation is fully expressed in it. Susan is also threatened by images of herself, and the moment of release comes only after she ? now back out of character as Nicky ? has destroyed the distorted self-image and the inner demon (represented by the 'Phantom') before entering room 47.X sees herself as both murderer and victim. She has been hurt by events, some beyond her control, and she is angry about her husband's lack of communication about his infertility. She feels accused by the wife of her ex-lover (although we don't know whether this woman actually threatened her). But she is also the perpetrator of her own downfall, as represented by the Polish prostitute, who carries out the murder in Lodz. The fact that Susan is stabbed low down in her stomach might also reflect the loss of the baby, perhaps through miscarriage ('she had a hole in her vagina wall').X's fantasies include an obsession about time ('9.45', 'after midnight') and numbers ('47') but the actual circumstances to which these refer are not known to us: we can only speculate. Whether or not she is Polish, or was married to a Polish husband, is also uncertain. However, we do not need to know all the biographical details to feel the anguish suffered by X.A tragic extra-marital affair, the breakdown of a marriage and the loss of a child are not uncommon events, although they certainly don't happen to everyone. They haven't happened to me. Yet Inland Empire gives its audience a powerful insight into the emotional state of someone living with the bereavement and despair involved.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>A movie that should have made history...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>They say that it is just as likely for a fly to land on sh!t as it is pie, and well the same can be said for Lynchian cinema. I have always hated reading critical responses of straight laced people and there views of David Lynch films. If you have no imagination and ability to wonder without a guided tour, than Lynch will never be your chef.Inland Empire suffers without this kind of help from the casual summer blockbuster fan. If you look back at the career of David Lynch you can see where he gets things very right and when he fails miserably. Two examples that come to mind are Wild at Heart and Fire Walk with me. In these instances it seemed like Lynch lost track of story telling and got caught up in the on set moment to moment artistry. Both examples struggle because the individual parts were stronger than the sum of the elements combined.Inland Empire unfortunately, is just that all over again. Wonderful in its moment to moment scenes and nearly unwatchable as a whole. If you were one of the many Lynch fans to eagerly jump for the midnight release of the DVD than you can see this point exactly. Watch the deleted material for Inlnd Empire, you will be treated to nearly an hour and a half of scenes that were cut. The funny thing is that much of this material is better than the film and much more pertinent to the story telling. It made me want to re-edit the film and create another story entirely and strange enough with this additional material it would be possible.I just think Lynch works best when he has a filter to run his ideas through and keep his story telling somewhat on track. As for Inland Empire, it is a film that I love and hate equally, a film with all of the potential and Lynchian elements to be haunting and beautiful. I catch myself watching this film hoping each time that it will get better or seem different. This is one of those films I really want to like. Inland Empire even in 2010 still continues do draw me in like a whirl pool of obsession, but in the end I feel like I just sat through a 3 hour film school, critiquing the film mostly for what it could have been. Unfortunately the real gems of this picture are in the moment to moment scenes and not the film as a whole. David Lynch and Laura Dern should never work together again, they have they might have the Polish gypsy curse.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>David Lynch unchained</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I saw this movie for the second time a couple of nights ago while watching it with my dad and I can tell you that watching it a second time brought not too many revelations of what the actual PLOT was but it did strengthen my idea that this is David Lynch's masterpiece.To paraphrase what my dad said, David Lynch takes things that happen to people that are traumatic and expresses them in an abstract and insane way. The reason for this is that when someone is going through something life-altering or traumatic they are usually driven to the brink of insanity. But we've been taught by society to bury this deep inside of ourselves. David Lynch, as an artist, expresses these feelings.There really is hardly a way to bring spoilers out in a review of this movie because everything is disconnected, yet perfectly interwoven, non-sequitor, yet the obvious replies, disturbing and beautiful, broken and assembled. It is so huge that it is nearly impossible to swallow it in one gulp, even two, as I learned. I have read that David Lynch had to cut several of his other movie's running time down for one reason or another, but as David Lynch discovered the freedom of Digital Video, he freed himself to pack everything he's ever wanted to do into a folding, amazing, mammoth of a movie. Imagine if an urban epic like Paul Thomas Anderson's Magnolia was stripped to it's bare emotion and then buried deep within the shadow of your psyche. That is what Inland Empire is.The movie begins with a prologue about a shady Polish prostitution syndicate. It then shifts to Laura Dern as the actress Nikki Grace. She has an affair with the co-star of a movie she is working on which is a remake of a cursed movie. After that who knows what it is about in terms of plot. The only thing one can be sure of is that actions do have consequences. The scenes in which Laura Dern plays the other side of Nikki Grace, Sue, and tells Sue's life stories to the psychiatrist(or whoever that guy is) are some of the best examples of Laura Dern's superb acting ability, and this is the best role I have seen her in and she deserved that Best Actress nomination David Lynch campaigned for with a cow back in 2007.The movie takes you on a journey through total darkness and then ends with a redemption so excellently executed that although it reveals nothing as to what the plot is about on the surface, it raises you from a place of complete hopelessness and despair to a fantastical gladness that everyone(seemingly) made it through their night terrors unscathed.And, on top of all that, the movie has the most creative end credits I've ever seen.Final Rating 10 out of 10, this is David Lynch's masterpiece.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Lynch's Masterpiece</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Inland Empire is truly an indescribable piece of art. David Lynch travels beyond the boundaries of what is considered film, much like Stanley Kubrick's 2001. Lynch perfects his surrealistic dreamscape style with Inland Empire.Lynch uses his cinematography as a means to transport the viewer into another world, giving the film an almost documentary look to it, through its use of lighting and framing of the subjects. Inland Empire is a recollection of a nightmare you dreamt the night before, vague fragmented memories, and blurred, terrifying images.What makes Inland Empire so memorizing and horrifying to watch is the nonsensical chaos of it all, the only thing you can be certain about in the uncertainty of what you'll see next. Your imagination is in a constant frenzy, frantically searching for something to latch onto and regain control. Lynch will occasionally utilize this thought process to his advantage, lulling the viewer into a feeling of safety, only to have it melt away seconds later as he escorts you deeper into his Hell.The film's score also plays a key role in creating the atmosphere, providing a dirty, grainy feel, the sharp noises making you feel uneasy, even in the moments of calm.Inland Empire is David Lynch's masterpiece; it's pure, unrestrained, psychological horror, it's 3 hours of hopelessness and terror, and you can't stop watching no matter how hard you try.</fullReview>

		<username>Joshua Brown (graceofbaal)</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>David Lynch Unplugged</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>David Lynch is an interesting character. He's been everywhere in the film department: from underground cult ("Eraserhead"), to classic watch-in-high school ("The Elephant Man"), to big-budget Hollywood ("Mulholland Dr."). But this new one, "Inland Empire", can't quite be categorized at all. It's surreal, experimental, it has big-budget movie stars, but yet it has all the makings of a cheap independent film. Perhaps that's the beauty of Lynch.I refuse to say how I interpret the film, because it's different for everyone. A clean cut explanation, as Lynch would say, destroys the experience of watching it. That said, don't go into this film with an expectation of "figuring it out", not only because it perhaps doesn't have a defined meaning, but also because over-analyzing a Lynch film is futile and destroys the world which you're about to be transported into. Some movies are made for pure entertainment (most these days are, whether it be to scare you, make you laugh, etc), while others are made with an underlying message (political, social, or whatever). Lynch does neither. What he does is create a different, surreal world for you to live in for the duration of the film. Sometimes this world is funny, or sometimes it's highly unsettling to the point of being terrifying, but that's the Lynchian world.That being said, I will say that this movie is his most non-linear, and is his hardest film to follow. But you don't have to follow it. Because it's not about narrative, it's about experience. We as humans naturally try to follow things in an understanding, linear way. But you gotta break yourself of that habit, and watch "Inland Empire", not to understand it, but to experience it."Inland Empire" is not for everyone. If your idea of a good movie is "Pirates of the Caribbean", or "Live Free or Die Hard", then you may not be ready for this film. Although, it's hard to say at all who would like or dislike it, since even some hard-core fans of Lynch dislike it. But "Inland Empire" is pure cinema, and David Lynch is one of the few filmmakers in this day and age who I believe are making true films.Anyways, if you do choose to see "Inland Empire", refuse any interpretation someone tries to give, and go into it cold turkey. Get yourself a drink, maybe a snack (although, this isn't quite the popcorn type of movie), watch it at night or in a dark room, and make sure there are no disturbances. Then just sit back, and let Lynch take the wheel. He'll take you into his world, and love it or hate it, you can say you've been there.Rating: 10/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Snipping The Thread, Lynch Lets His Film Implode Into A Completely Unaware Mess</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I don't and never have disliked David Lynch as a filmmaker. I like Blue Velvet very much. I think that is one of the most original and interesting movies of the 1980s or '90s. I like Mulholland Drive very very much. It was a very interesting, atmospheric, brilliant journey from the dream state to consciousness to dream and so on. But you see, despite all of the confusion and perplexity that was caused by many of Lynch's films, they were all held together by a thread of sensibility that made them coherent. Inland Empire begins that way and then decides to snip the thread.I'm not sure how much I like digital video cameras being such great resources for professional film-making these days, because that means that anybody can make a movie and thus it will be harder to discern the newcomers from talented to pure fluff material to self- indulgent to plain muddle-minded. I don't mean to be harsh. It's just that a lot of American movies are quite bad, and I feel that it's easy to mistake a particularly ambitious person or really someone who grew up wealthy with a lot of clout as the ones to choose to be our next individual new waves and generations that will further develop cinema when there are truly talented ones despite whether or not they have clout or business acumen. However, I found myself actually very pleased to see that a large-scale film by a name director could be made by way of digital video. It shows that movies can get somewhere in that medium and that Generation Y hasn't just been jerking off this whole time.I also thought Laura Dern gave a very impressive performance that takes hairpin turns sharper than nearly any other role I can recall, and she sucks up the character on the page and lets herself turn whatever shade she's just absorbed. Jeremy Irons is the other highlight of the cast, serving as the most refreshingly normal character and being very very enjoyably comfortable in his own skin in the role he plays, as if the character is very much like him.I am also very intrigued by the story as it opens and begins twisting and meshing into a very cerebral something else. However! Somewhere between the recollection of the family of rabbit people in sitcom form at the beginning awaiting connection to the rest of the events and Laura Dern's violent bursts out of character and into a series of deviant, angry, shy, false, and real personalities, I am severed from the tractor beam between the audience and the movie. The cast, though, still seems to know what they're doing, so the film either does mean something or they're giving doubly good performances by acting as if they are acting, fooling even the filmmakers and the audience as well as becoming someone else.In the end, when one is recalling the film, one only recalls a film that is more of a compilation of the director's work into a collage more than a film. When you're really watching, you know that there is a story being told, an actual story, but within the spinning wheels of Lynch's compulsion to be deliberately strange and cryptic in a way where he's giggling to himself and looking over his shoulder as he's writing it at the keyboard, the attention of the audience is released somewhat, at least to the point where we're now watching in a completely objective way because we're being left out. I'm starting to flirt with the idea that Lynch could be an indulgent filmmaker who enjoys toying with his audiences. I hope I'm proved wrong soon.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Lynch's most beautiful and haunting film yet...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Inland Empire is without a doubt one of the most bizarre, yet beautiful David Lynch films that I've ever seen. It also had one of the most interesting plots I've seen in a Lynch film. Understandably, a lot of people aren't going to like this movie because it is far more fractured than the typical Lynch film, which makes his other work seem completely straightforward, and that is definitely a feat. Plus, it's quite long and most people barely have enough patience to get through an hour and a half of a typical Lynch-esquire story line. It was also completely terrifying. There were a lot of very amazing psychological scare scenes. I noticed that Lynch used some of the same Penderecki score that was used in Stanley Kubrick's The Shining, which was a lovely touch. Music like that should be utilized far more in horror and psychological suspense films. Another interesting addition was the behind the scenes featurette on the 2nd disc. It shows that David Lynch is a very passionate artist and filmmaker who knows EXACTLY what he wants and demands it from his cast and crew. He gets right in there and very enthusiastically describes everything he wants from his actors. There were a few interesting scenes where he loses his patience with Laura Dern. Ironically, some of their banter reminded me of the behind the scenes of the movie The Shining and the relationship between Stanley Kubrick and Shelley Duvall, only not quite to that degree. Regardless, it is definitely the type of movie that needs to be watched again and again in order to catch little bits that weren't noticed before. The bottom line is that David Lynch is a master storyteller and approaches his films in a completely unique and interesting way. This is why they seem so fractured and end up confounding so many people. However, if you are willing to open yourself up to it and have patience, you will be rewarded with a truly wonderful and haunting story that will stay with you for a long time after.</fullReview>

		<date>2007-03-16</date>
		<username>Ndirsch11</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>"Inland Empire" a bold work of artistic vision</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The latest film from legendary director David Lynch (Blue Velvet, Mulholland Dr.) invites the viewer to abandon all preconceived notions and expectations concerning film as a medium of storytelling. We're invited to be like Alice in Wonderland, tumbling down the rabbit hole into a world strange and unfamiliar. The world of "Inland Empire" exists entirely within the subconscious: a world populated by Freudian symbols where nothing is what it appears to be and time and space have no meaning. This is a world of dreams in which all semblance of plot and narrative is thrown out the window. "Inland Empire" works like a dream because it is a dream. Don't worry if this doesn't make any sense. Believe me when I say that this is probably the strangest movie ever made."Inland Empire" is the ultimate vanity project. For those unfamiliar with the director, David Lynch, all I can say is that he's unlike any other director working today. Lynch is the Salvador Dali of filmmakers: his medium is surrealism and his canvas is film. He's one of the most talented directors of his generation but also one of the most frustrating. The ultimate question concerning his artistic merit is this: are his films simply weird for weird sake or is there something more to it than meets the eye? In the case of "Inland Empire" I choose the latter. A Lynch film should be viewed as an abstract work of art: interpretations may vary but the feelings conveyed within are clear.The plot (what plot?) of "Inland Empire" is difficult to follow and more difficult to explain. We're introduced to a character named Nikki Grace (Laura Dern), an actress living in Hollywood who's hoping to be cast in a new movie. As she awaits a call from her agent, Nikki gets paid a visit by a mysterious woman who might be some kind of fortune teller. She warns Nikki that she might die if she gets the part and makes vague insinuations about other unfortunate events. When Nikki finally gets the lead in the movie, she finds herself falling in love with her co-star, Devon Berk (Justin Theroux). To complicate matters, the script is about an adulterous affair between the two leads. The problem for Nikki is that she finds her real life mirroring that of the character she's playing in a fictional film. Also, it is later revealed that the film she's shooting is a remake of a doomed Polish production which remained unfinished due to the murder of the two stars. The production of the movie appears to be cursed.The rest of the movie plays like a surrealist nightmare in which the line between thought and reality are never clear. The entire movie may, in fact, be a dream of Nikki's where she's forced to confront her own fears and insecurities as an actress. Or is it someone else's dream about an actress playing a woman in a film? Or is "Inland Empire" actually a movie within a movie: a film about the difficult of film-making? There's no easy answer here."Inland Empire" is full of startling images and unforgettable moments. At several points the film cuts to a group of talking rabbits who seem to inhabit the set of some bad television sitcom. The rabbits talk in deadpan voices as a laugh track interrupts the dialogue. Later a group of prostitutes cast in shadow recount the horrors and abuse they face on a daily basis. This scene is interrupted when the prostitutes randomly break out into song and dance as they perform "The Locomotion." No explanation is ever given as to why.Trying to figure out "Inland Empire" is like trying to solve a Rubik's Cube: no matter how hard you try to line things up, they never fit. Some viewers are likely to find "Inland Empire" an exasperating and frustrating experience. At three hours in length, "Inland Empire" is an intellectually challenging film. My recommendation is that you submit yourself to the film's epic weirdness: this is a puzzle that's worth figuring out.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>bigheadfilms</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>lynch gone verite?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It's an abstract work, and should be reviewed as such; (as opposed to 'Wah, this doesn't make any sense.') Lynch utilizes the freedom of digital video to riff on the themes near and dear to his heart. The visual language is different from film, but this is a good thing; the results when a film maker tries to make video look like film are usually pretty terrible. Lynch chose the Sony pd170 camera because it is the best camera in low light, so he can just dive right into shadows. The thing can shoot in 1 lux, or the amount of light one candle emits from about 1 yard away, as opposed to a pro-sumer HD cam which needs about 7 lux to "see". The film is composed of many vignettes on the same themes: identity, and the malleability thereof; the psychological dangers of acting; voyeurism; mystery; seeing; dream logic; light and shadow, literally and metaphorically. What it all means is secondary to what it makes one feel. Its a quite visceral visual and aural experience. Lynch is I think the best film maker for showing the viewer rather than telling him. In other words, this work of art is best executed in the cinematic medium; it wouldn't work as a play or a novel or a painting. The performances (not just Dern's) are rich and multi-layered, as we now know Lynch views the universe vis a vis his practice of Transcendental Meditation and the interconnectedness of all things. He has said openly that large pieces of the film were written moments before filming and that the actors didn't know the whole picture when acting any particular scene. (A lot like life, eh?) Bottom line: this is one of Lynch's best, and probably the best work of art yet created in this medium.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Brian Roan (LastAttack)</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>I saw it, and walked out of the theater. I fear, however, that I will be forever trying to walk out of the movie.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>If I told you that David Lynch's Inland Empire was the best movie I have seen in the past few years, I would be lying. If I told you it was the worst I had seen in that same time frame, I would be lying. If I said I understood the film, I would be lying. If I said I was completely baffled by it, I would be lying. The film itself defies - and laughs in the face of - explanation or categorization. I sat down knowing a few Lynch movies (Blue Velvet, Mulholland Drive, Twin Peaks: Fire Walk With Me) and left knowing those movies again and every movie ever made before them. For three hours I felt trapped in my seat, a viewer, a watcher, someone on the outside, someone on the inside, someone being watched and someone taunted and thanked all at once. I struggled to grasp the meaning of what was going on and the more I did the more I felt my control over the movie and its narrative slipping away. Around the time a group of people began singing and dancing to The Locomotion I gave up and realized I was lost in a turgid sea of images and stories, and that trying to swim with or against them would only cause me to drown in them. This is the point of the movie, I think. To wash us in our narrative expectations - but not just from one movie, from all movies - and to shock us with sudden jolts away from these expectations. Are the performances great? I cannot say. I couldn't tell when they were acting, when they were acting like people acting, and when they were simply people. I may sound lost, or pretentious, but that is the only way you can react to this movie. You will struggle with your own ability to explain just what you have seen, because what just happened was on a level of storytelling and movie-making that is not even in existence. It is cerebral, a barrage of images and actions and motions made to impress on us an idea and a world. I saw it, and walked out of the theater. I fear, however, that I will be forever trying to walk out of the movie.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>I have just seen David Lynch's "Inland Empire", and first off must recommend to Lynch and friends that he arrange at once for a special international viewing at the US Embassy in Kabul, Afghanistan -- there are videophiles there who screen cult films and who will appreciate the Afghan national premiere of this film, following from their experience viewing the Afghan national premieres of Eraserhead in 2004, and Blue Velvet and Lost Highway in 2005. Sitcom rabbits, surreally posed as if on the 1950s set of 'I Love Lucy', with laughtrack included; the inherent symbolism is profound, and coherent. As codebreakers search for the enigmatic key to read new codes, so the rabbits must be read to view this film. Clearly, synthetic chemicals were involved in the making of this film, and I'm not talking about the digital video 'stock', as it were; the key to realizing this is the repeated presence of the rabbits. Lynch is clearly indulging in homage to that seminal cinematic masterpiece, 'Night of the Lupus' (not to be confused with Night of the Living Dead, nor Night Porter, nor even Night People, although the subliminal associations and references to these films are prolific and clearly self-evident). Reliable sources reveal the Golden Globes wanted to vote this three-hour opus 'Best Picture Viewed in the Reflection of a Automobile Hood Ornament', and who am I to disagree with this visceral groundswell opinion. I can only say that in future midnight presentations of "Eraserhead" at the Waverly/ 8th Street Playhouse venues in Greenwich Village, 'Inland Empire' must replace the venerable 'Asparagus' as the short before the main feature. For those who have seen 'Empire', this can be nothing other than self explanatory.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>It has something to do with the telling of time</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>First off let it be known that I've seen this movie six times. INLAND EMPIRE absolutely demands multiple viewings. The first time out I felt the same way as the negative reviewers: the film is too long, too confusing, and too demanding. About halfway into it I gave up on trying to "follow" the story and just enjoyed the imagery. When to false ending happened (Lost Girl embraces with Nikki/Sue) I know that I had missed something, but I wasn't sure what. Then the there's the stupendous, exhilarating finale when the characters come together and dance to the Nina Simone song. The title of the film flashed on the screen one more time, and the three hours were over. I KNEW that I had missed something, and in subsequent viewings I began to really sense. Trying to experience it as a normal movie does not work. INLAND EMPIRE occurs in three different cycles of time: linear, circular and parallel. These cycles frequently cross over each other and blur into one another. Just like in one's dream, you are in many places at once without moving. I wonder about the audience members who walked out of the theater grumbling that it was pretentious. I wanted to ask them: are your dreams pretentious? Is your brain pretentious? Fifty years from now, people will look back on INLAND EMPIRE as being the movie that changed EVERYTHING about the art form. It is an utter masterpiece. By the way, you don't know what the deal is with the rabbits? Go back and re-read Alice in Wonderland and Alice through the Looking Glass.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>An American Treasure</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>With this superb, surrealist epic, David Lynch, and his fellow visionary, actress Laura Dern have firmly established themselves among the truly great geniuses of the moving image. Lynch has now earned his rightful place alongside Kurosawa, Fellini, Bergman, Bunuel. Not so much a "movie" in the more traditional sense, as a surrealist work employing the devices of cinema, "Inland Empire" burrows further into the viewer's unconscious, bringing him/her further towards a sensory, mystical experience of one's own cosmology (as channeled through Lynch's) than almost any other picture before it. The movie watches you as much as you watch it. It is psychosis filmed from the inside; quantum-reality dreams and nightmares demonstrated, inscrutably, before one's eyes and ears. Lynch has previously attempted to film journeys through the rabbit-hole, with extremely entertaining and thought-provoking results. This time he made it through, and you are invited to see (and hear) the resulting footage in glorious, un-glossy Digital Video. I find it only tepidly interesting whether Mr. Lynch can actually account verbally for how this is done. If one believes, as I do, that certain great artists are in touch with larger realities than their own everyday, reductionist, analytical minds as they create their masterpieces, one should have no problem sinking back into a theater seat to take this 3-hour trip through the intricate, un- mapable, un-nameable dark passageways of a cinematic visionary's mind. If you dare, I recommend tuning your mind to receive this piece. Perhaps not a risk free venture - especially for those unaccustomed to such mind-travel - but then, visionary art never has been. Moo!</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Empire built on sand</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A perfect example of everything I find baffling about David Lynch, namely a man of obvious genius and vision who chooses to waste it on such mindless, masturbatory rubbish.The moments of beauty -- the needle on the record player, the nightmarish rabbits, the crying woman in the room -- are buried amid an empty blur of random images and nonsensical turns in the bend that lead only to exasperation and puzzlement. The characters are valueless and empty, allowing one no access to internal workings outside of disturbing visuals that have all the personal feeling of a schizophrenic's disassociated delusions.One does not glimpse into Laura Dern's soul and its too-human wrestling with a curse, or personal guilt and fear, one merely lifts the sod to take in what lay underneath -- the bugs and worms roiling in the ground, a well-known, and facile, Lynch motif from Blue Velvet.Like the insects, Dern's delusions are supposed to have deep meaning for the viewer. In Blue Velvet, the idea was the rot underneath the suburban dream; in Inland Empire, the visual hodgepodge is, one can assume, supposed to signify the curse, and perhaps various moral concepts -- guilt for her affair, fear of her husband, empathy with the other women etc. However, like the insects, the delusions are glib and too on-the-nose. They are also incredibly distracting in their broken and jagged chaos. One is reminded of the phrase from the statue in Amelie: "The fool looks at a finger that points at the sky." Sadly, the fool watching Inland Empire is left with little else to gaze upon; the droning sludge of the film's three hours (!?) makes even gazing at the finger an exercise in sheer will. There is also the possibility that what Lynch's finger is pointing at is simply an empty sky.An irritating film and a good reminder of why I feel Lynch is one of the most overrated and consistently disappointing directors alive today. Perhaps I am simply one of those unfortunate souls who is simply not smart enough to get Lynch. The fact I feel little in the way of loss or regret for this status tells me all I need to know.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>My Least Favorite Lynch Film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>An actress's perception of reality becomes increasingly distorted as she finds herself falling for her co-star in a remake of an unfinished Polish production that was supposedly cursed.David Lynch is an inspiration for those who want to do their own thing. He started with some creepy, surreal short films, moved on to the bizarre "Eraserhead" and has made numerous strange films since, seemingly with little regard for what critics or box office receipts say. While he has done some stories that border on normal, there is always an element of the bizarre, and trying to make sense of a plot in a Lynch film is an intellectual landmine. I fell in love with Lynch after seeing "Lost Highway" and "Twin Peaks", but find it hard to justify my love."Inland Empire" continues the madness. Opening with a beautiful shot of a record player in black and white, then of a couple making love... we are soon greeted by a family of anthropomorphized rabbits, and two gentlemen having a discussion in some foreign language, apparently Polish. How any of these things are connected is unclear thus far, and that is only the first ten minutes of a three hour film. How well they make sense by the end is hard to say.The film goes "normal" after that, through we enter a world with odd camera angles, mismatched close-ups, and conversations that do not flow... a murder accusation, an agent on the telephone... and all words spoken in an unusual manner. Almost in an amateur way, but clearly professionals trying to be amateur, with emotions not matching the events. And then there is William H. Macy as an announcer...On top of already being a lengthy and interesting film, the DVD comes with a second disc with several hours of supplemental material. My only complaint is that, despite all this, the film only has subtitles available in French. I like subtitles. Maybe I am in the minority, but as a visual learner, I follow a story better when I can read along. And I cannot read French well.Lynch veteran Laura Dern stars (and co-produces), which is beneficial to the film, because she knows exactly what sort of weirdness Lynch is looking for. I think the film fails, or at least falls short, though. The running time is a bit too long and it is hard to stay focused for this length. Also, the film just does not seem to have enough. While starting out strong, it gets slow and monotonously as it carries on, and any intelligent point hidden in there is missed by the audience's boredom. I appreciated the inclusion of the randomness, such as the "Locomotion", but of all the Lynch films I have seen (which is many) this is my least favorite.If you must see every David Lynch film, see this film. If you can survive without that goal, do not see this film. I cannot recommend it to anyone except his biggest fans because everyone else will be lost, bored and leave with a lower opinion of the man than he deserves.</fullReview>

		<username>johnnyboyz</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Annoyingly, Inland Empire Implodes Emphatically the more it wears on; offering a few shocks at items we cannot follow plus very few instances of narrative clarity.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I think David Lynch's Inland Empire is interesting for about an hour, but after about a third or so of its runtime and after we've jumped from one thing to another and back to something else before finishing on an item which I think was supposed to tie it all together but I'm not sure, the film looses sight. Here is a piece that will leap from people dressed in rabbit suits ironing and talking on the telephone whilst acting in a TV sitcom; to a look at somebody experiencing unrequited love; to a bunch of what I presume to be Polish gangsters doing what it is they do; to the tale about production of a film adapting a cursed fable and then onto something else entirely different in the slight hope you're still keeping up. Perhaps that's the point; perhaps nobody can keep up, much like in the vein of, who I think is the lead character in Laura Dern's Nikki Grace, cannot keep up with her world around her which is rapidly spiralling out of control; maybe that's the point: maybe it's all one big viewpoint of a film within a film from someone severely deranged.Whatever it is, it is interesting if only for a while. There is a point you zone out, a point in which everything collapses in on itself by way of the amount of stuff that Lynch continues to pile on top of you. Just as I was starting to figure out why it was that a certain rabbit in a mock TV sitcom was sitting on a sofa in a specific manner was linked to a Polish prostitute and the stalker on the set of a film production which is having a second crack at getting made, was I bombarded with a scene in which I was forced into calculating what binded a jam-like substance vomiting clown had in common with a troupe of dancing bikini-clad women.You can call it avant-garde, you can call it what you like; but the underlying message I got out of Inland Empire was that the world is a strange and disorientating place, and that it's alright to exist within it and be a little confused now and again. The film begins with some sort of footage resembling a mock-undercover trafficking documentary, the kind that sees the faces of those that are guilty blurred out as hidden cameras capture all the evidence the authorities need to put the people on screen in prison. From here, we jump to the crying eye of a Polish girl as she watches a television, in what seems to be a self-referential sequence linked to the capturing of one's gaze as she sits looking transfixed and evoking emotion over a number of rabbits pottering about on the TV screen, talking out of sync and evoking warm laughter from a diegetic audience despite not actually doing anything. Later, the room will revealed to be sort of connected to the wider (real?) world when Dern's character seems to stumble across it ? is the Polish girl aware of, or indeed actually watching, the plight of Dern's character within Inland Empire? Does that render Dern's fictional character a fictional character within a fictional piece? Most probably and not at all both at once.Dern's character is an actress called Nikki Grace, at least initially. When we first see her in the place she plies her trade, that being a studio set, we get some deliberately soppy romance genre-infused music as it becomes apparent her next project is an adaptation of a supposedly doomed piece of Polish literature; doomed in the sense it has an uncanny reputation in having whatever acting talent that plays its lead roles die mysteriously. Nikki must spend some time with actor and co-worker for the piece Devon Berk (Theroux), someone she unexpectedly falls in love with. At least I'm pretty sure this is what happens. There are only very few occasions Lynch informs you of what's actually going on, or provides you with stone wall occasions in which you think you know what's going on. Nikki's period of panic as she comes to terms with what she's done in spending the night with Devon is highlighted when the aforementioned sequence in a blood red drenched bedroom evokes terror amongst the audience when generic sensibilities of mockumentary and romance clash with an approach of pure avant-garde.Rather depressingly, Inland Empire is, I think, border-line nonsense which, in a somewhat merciful manner, does feel like it's actually about something at sporadic, intermittent times. There are occasions the film leads you into thinking you've sort of cracked what's going on, but, rather annoyingly, I think it takes more pleasure in pulling out the rug from beneath you more than it does in delivering a comprehensive film-watching experience for you. Sure, there were times I was unnerved but what I was unnerved at, I have no idea - any film can plunge the audience into the unknown with a digital video aesthetic; low-level lighting plus off the wall content and get a desired effect of sheer panic born out of ambiguity. I'm torn with the piece - I didn't hate Inland Empire; I cannot recommend it but then again, I cannot explain it although there are those that cannot either but give the go ahead in seeing it anyway. It's an ambitious piece; a piece that feigns as if you need your head tuned in at all times but might very well work just as well if the viewer is less attentive that first appears. I don't ask for a film-going experience to spell everything out for me, but I ask for a degree of clarity; and in Inland Empire there simply isn't that degree. It got people talking upon its first release in 2006, and it'll continue to get people talking more in time, but I wonder if anyone actually knows just what it is they're all talking about.</fullReview>

		<username>howie73</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>A towering achievement</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>After countless viewings I have come to the conclusion that INLAND EMPIRE is not quite as masterful as Mulholland Drive. Why? INLAND EMPIRE (hereafter IE) is more abstract than its predecessor but not as intuitive, as demonstrated by the plurality of theories, most of which are wildly divergent. Conversely, there was a consensus (most of the time) about the dream aspect of Mulholland Drive (hereafter MD) and the supposed back story, which was more transparent than the one Lynch deliberately obscures in IE.While MD had a character we could empathize with in the guise of the Betty/Diane division, the dual identity of Nikki/Sue presented in IE is harder to grasp. Nikki doesn't endear us like Betty did in her wide-eyed innocence; while Sue is presented as a deeply disturbed, embittered woman, lacking in any redeeming features.The story of IE is not as intuitive to grasp as MD's. Little is known about the 'real' story and how it relates to the Lost Girl and Poland vis a vis Sue's consciousness, and we don't really know whose viewpoint is privileged. In MD, we knew Diane was the central stream of consciousness; in IE, it's deeply ambiguous whether or not the Lost Girl is projecting her life to Nikki/Sue or if it's the other way around. Lynchian fans may grasp this ambiguity as part of the film's appeal, but for viewers who like to find meaning(s) after countless viewings, it's deeply frustrating and unrewarding. Surely film should reward any viewer's patience, not stifle it? Technically, IE's digital video was a brave move for a director known for his visual flourishes and painterly approach to film. Mulholland Drive, without a doubt, is a visual feast and shows a masterful grasp of cinematography courtesy of Peter Demming. In IE the results are impressive but sadly inconsistent. The decision to film with hand-held DV cameras is well handled but there are times when there is a sense of technical ineptitude, where the camera is too jerky or out of focus in low-light, most notably in the torch scenes and the night scenes on Hollywood and Vine where Nikki sees Doris from afar hiding behind a tree for the first time. These are minor gripes. Only the most technically-minded person would notice themIn terms of editing, IE nearly matches MD. I can't think of any bad editing instances in MD, but in IE, there is a clear one that I was amazed Lynch/Mary Sweeney allowed. The edit in question is between the Nikki ('Damn this sound likes dialogue from our script") scene and the one in which Devon is told about the idiocy of the gypsy curse by Nikki's agent. When I saw this in the cinema, the transition was very abrupt, intensified by the music in the Nikki scene being cut to accommodate Nikki's agent in mid-flow. I sense this was a mistake and not intended, or perhaps it was, but it didn't feel right. This might be a minor gripe, but I expected perfection. Morever, the typically absurd Bucky J scene may provide light relief but does it really add anything to the film? The gibberish spoken by the bald-headed man on set was also baffling but something I half-expected, given Lynch's propensity for obscurantism.I also think MD is more of an honest film than IE. MD takes us on a journey through a tortured world of a deeply disturbed woman who cannot find salvation or the light, only the brief hope of unrequited love, brutally cut by the netherworld that is Silencio. Conversely, IE takes us on a torturous journey where salvation is about self-discovery and finding the light out of the darkness. It may be more positive than MD's silencio, but sweet it surely isn't. Was there anything sweet about Sue's life? The surprising credits sequence further mystifies the film. Why Sinnerman? Do they matter? Are they really part of the film. I wish he ahd ended with Polish poem on a sombre note. Sweet felt like a reckless riposte to a film that was even darker than Mulholland Drive and didn't feel quite right yet as an in-joke it might have worked.I enjoyed INLAND EMPIRE a lot. I still think it's a brilliant film, in the sense that it's Lynch's most ambitious film to date and one where he brings his dreamstate to narrative without the obvious conceit of the omnipresent dreamer. It develops and exceeds ideas in his earlier films. I just wish the film had ended before the credit sequence. To close, IE includes many scenes that exceed MD, but as a totality, it falls just short but not by that much. A towering achievement nevertheless.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>If you don't get it... get out of the cinema and watch reality TV</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>David Lynch's latest film comprises several different but related "realities." Those who say it's just a jumble of random scenes aren't paying attention. The numerous visual rhymes (for instance: a man with a huge ketchup stain on his belly in one scene, a woman dripping blood from her screwdriver-impaled stomach in another) and lines repeated by different characters in different contexts, like verbal leitmotifs, make up a complex web of interrelated, fractured narratives. Some say it's like a cubist painting, but it's more like a hypercube. Inland Empire is a mutlidimensional, trans-temporal maze, bewildering but endlessly intriguing. As usual with Lynch, dream logic dominates. But if Eraserhead is like one prolonged nightmare, Inland Empire is like an entire night of interconnected dreams condensed into three hours. There's nothing here that's as thrilling and strangely moving as the Club Silencio scene in Mulholland Drive, but overall I think I like Inland Empire a bit better. It's much more complex and even more amenable to multiple viewings. Although I doubt the film will ever quite reveal itself as a surprisingly understandable story the way Mulholland Drive does. Inland Empire is audacious, crazy and daring. Unfortunately, a lot of people, including a lot of Lynch fans, resent the film for it. But to me it adds up to a remarkable, truly innovative achievement.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>This is not a film (which is not a bad thing)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In my opinion IE is more (much more!!) an artistic construction than a film. It should be projected in a Biennale art show and it would be a smash hit! But films are about stories. Stories which can be told in tops 1 paragraph. The way to tell a story makes a film a masterpiece or just crap! I 've read 6 different attempts to describe the plot of IE. They all failed. And they failed not because the people who wrote them were idiots, but because they had to describe a modern art exhibit. This is a job for art historians and critics. And they have to be very good!!! When you get out of the theater you get the right feeling but you can't explain it, why you feel this way and if that was the point of the film. Film-making is an art for the masses. If it was only for the cultivated people, there wouldn't be any theaters but only small rooms for 10 people and the ticket would be 500 EURO. This fact doesn't work only for blockbuster crap. We all have seen many films which were very spiritual and cultural and they had many tickets. But these movies worked on 2 levels. The first was a story for all the people to understand and the second was about the meaning of the film, which wasn't always the same for all. IE didn't have the first level, and it sultan't be criticized as a film. Excuse my bad English...</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Seems kinda lazy to "hate" this movie...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>...and so many people seem to capital letter H-A-T-E it. And then throw those over-used phrases around "pretentious" and "pseudo-intellectual"... when in reality it is absolutely INTELLECTUAL, not pseudo... Some things in this world ARE legitimately intellectual, ya know...I have a friend, who is very prominent in Hollywood, who I think sums up Hollywood's attitude toward David Lynch. When I was telling him how much I liked Inland Empire even though it was still confusing to me, he only had this to say, "Like Lynch himself even knows what it means. Get a story. Loser." Ah, Hollywood... But like I said, to dismiss it out of hand just seems, well, DUMB. Clearly, this is not an ordinary linear plot with boy meets girl, boy loses girl, boy gets girl back -- that much can be agreed upon. So then CLEARLY we are challenged to try and understand it in a different manner. One reviewer said it is CUBISM applied to a movie script. That is an interesting theory and seems the most likely after I have given it much thought; and it's better than trying to decipher it all as some private code of Lynch's, full of hidden meanings, color codes, etc...From wikipedia: CUBISM -- "the painting of a normal scene but so that it is viewed from multiple views while the positions of some of the parts are rotated or moved so that it is odd looking and scrambled. In cubist artworks, objects are broken up, analyzed, and re-assembled in an abstracted form ? instead of depicting objects from one viewpoint, the artist depicts the subject from a multitude of viewpoints to represent the subject in a greater context. Often the surfaces intersect at seemingly random angles presenting no coherent sense of depth."Well, with regard to character, plot, timeline, action and dialogue this is indeed what seems to occur in Inland Empire... AND, brilliantly enough, it appears that Lynch then applies cubism to the relation of the actor to the role, the actor to the movie, the movie to ITSELF and finally the relationship of the viewer to the movie itself AND to the very movie theatre he finds himself sitting in!It seems to me that the director is presenting a brand new concept in film-making and is doing it in, believe it or not, the most DIRECT way he possibly could. How ELSE would you present a vision of filmic cubism?? In this light, Inland Empire is something that has never been done before and also seems to be the culmination of Lynch's career to date. As in 'masterpiece'!So I say: Brilliant! And kinda lazy to simply 'hate'...</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>The evil directors do</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Disciples of David Lynch will know that his signature form of presentation is to fashion an emblazoned phallus from celluloid and angrily thrust it into the bottom of his audience. He gets away with it principally on account of the fact that he has the psychological measure of his fans, those introspective ghouls who think mainstream is a synonym for conformist and would abhor the notion that they might be ordinary enough to enjoy such fare as this would run in direct opposition to the lies they've told themselves since their abused childhood. Just as Lynch packages pretencion as art, his acolytes wear whatever he throws at them as genius. For them it's a perfect fit ? the wonderful fusion of auteur and cinephile. In reality its closer to how the hence man in Robocop wears his own skin having been subsumed by toxic waste. If Lynch is a genius, it's fair to say that the people who eulogise his work are not.I foreground my comments on Inland Empire with this because your enjoyment of it will be pinned to where on the 'I love Dave scale' you'd put yourself. If you wear your love of impenetrable, kooky, obscure movies like a badge, a sort of layered intelligence by proxy, then you'll love what he's done here. If you think Lynch can't tell a story and covers himself by knitting little visi-poems together then you won't find anything in Empire to change your mind.What struck me as my knees became heavy across its unfeasibly long running time was that I was looking at very sharp, very visceral piece of cinema that simultaneously hated itself and its subject matter. It's a sort of anti-film, shot on poor quality DV in a 2 fingered 'f*ck off and take your hand off my breasts', kind of rebuke to the smoothness of conventional cinematography. It's the right tool for the job because it contributes to the jarring, garish sense of disjuncture that runs through the whole thing like the name of a seaside town in a stick of rock. Half way through I temporarily lost my mind. There's no conventional narrative, no plot as such, no characters in the classic sense, just this nightmarish stream of false consciousness that leaps from situation to situation, made even less pleasant by the ambient hum and stylus scratch that backgrounds most scenes. If you ate an entire slab of blue cheese, smoked a mushroom and went to bed early, this is what your dream would look like.Lynch's previous, Mulholland Drive, trod similar ground ? the Hollywood Dream as a dehumanising, corn fed nightmare ? an environment that defecated into it's victims mouths when the menu promised caviar. But whereas Drive was sort of beautiful as well as unsettling, Inland Empire is like watching an episode of 'you've been framed' fronted by Myra Hindley. It's bloody hard work; occasionally unbearable, sometimes unremittingly tedious and the stuff in-between takes perverse pleasure in being as obscure as humanly possible. If you stick with it to the end you realise there is a structure and it does have a sort of circular logic (hence the stylus maybe?) but it's also ridden with touches that suggest an inevitable question. Is Lynch having us on here or what? A family of rabbits, a bloke who talks gibberish while waving his finger at you, Mary Steenburgen, subtitled English, the locomotion ? sometimes Inland Empire is so conspicuously weird that it's almost impossible to take seriously. I'd like to think Lynch knows what it's all about ? that really would make him a genius. If he doesn't he's done well to convince so many that a searing intelligence shapes this shapeless camcorder evil.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>The art of David Lynch</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I've just done a one-hour trip to watch this?Wow! And I mean wow?What a phenomenal piece of cinema this is and I wonder: can you be so amazed by a movie without knowing what it is about? "Sit back, relax; you're on a David Lynch film", Laura Dern said on an interview. Her co-star Justin Theroux also explained that it had become a game for them to figure out what the movie was about while shooting: "I think that even David can't tell you", he added. Cinema, making cinema, above all things, is an art; and David Lynch is one of cinema's greater artists. Ironic however, is that, if you watch his last film and you don't know who he is, you'll probably think it is the work of an amateur. "Inland Empire" is the work of a professional and, as Lynch himself puts it, "a story about a woman in trouble". That's all you need to know about the plot; then you have to watch carefully.I met a man named Scott at the screening and he told me that the viewer only appreciates David Lynch's movies if they are seen in a movie theater. True, because you're experiencing the director's mind during three hours of pure cinematographic magic. And that's a mind that includes a large number of characters in the most twisted of stories, with the richest handling of digital camera you'll see in a long time.Some audiences that watched the picture complained because they said the digital camera image didn't look good on the big screen. False, it looks beautiful. With the cheap camera in his hand, Lynch takes all the risks generating a lot of movement and shooting directly into the actor's faces. He goes from light to dark, he appears, disappears and reappears, he crafts the music and the sound design so well that it becomes another main character (you really have to watch that), turning an actual scene into some kind of music video; he goes through all the moods of cinema which are, in some ways, the moods of life. His script, written whenever a new idea came to him in a period of two and half years, is more than ever a work of patience. During the whole film, after one character talks, the other stares intensely and waits at least five or ten seconds to answer; there are lots of insightful looks, there's a lot of contemplative silence that becomes dreamlike and consequently scary. The script has a lot of fear that can make you jump off your seat and it also has a slight subtle humor that works as a refreshing breeze of air when apparently no one can breathe. Lynch's total freedom becomes clear with his own editing, which we realize he controlled a lot, inserting his vision to the viewer in an even stronger way.However, there's another driving force inside "Inland Empire"; and that's Laura Dern's fantastic performance. To say fantastic is to find one appropriate word that summarizes her tour-de-force portrayal. She's on screen in every scene, the camera is on her face all of the time and she doesn't look at it; not even once in three hours. Of course she's not supposed to look at the camera, but her work is so focused that she doesn't even turn her eyes at times; she seems to be somewhere else than in Lynch's set, somewhere beyond. She takes her character beyond every possible level and she does it almost entirely on her own, with few answers from the rest of the individuals. Maybe another word to describe her act is unforgettable.And so is "Inland Empire", in the good or the bad way for anyone: an unforgettable experience and, as Scott said to me; a movie that will be playing itself in your head after five days?Or even more. One more thing: don't even bother on trying to figure out the 'mystery' or the meaning of the plot and you'll be alright.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Lynch's Biggest Homework Assignment</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>First, I would like to say that I did not make any final judgments until the credits began to roll. I did not enjoy this movie. I had already watched Lost Highway, and the collection of short films, so I figured I was prepared to watch another David Lynch film. I think the most problematic aspect of this motion picture is that it was in fact not film, but was shot on consumer miniDV. After working with it for several years, I loathe the format. If Inland Empire had just been a pilot, or a collection of test footage for an actual film, I could have appreciated that. However, I watched a three hour video that was to me, no better than a student "film" project. An 8mm or 16mm Inland Empire would have been much better.Through the whole thing, I was asking whether I should like it more than any other project of the same quality, just because David Lynch was the director. My decision was, "no." I enjoyed the challenges the he laid out in Lost Highway. I thought it was fun to be led on an endless trail of uncertainty. Perhaps I would have liked this movie more if it was shot on film, and if the production value was higher. However, after shooting three or four scenes, he should have changed course and switched to film.Although I dislike much of the movie and Mr. Lynch's decisions, I found a few points entertaining. I do not see it as a collection of random scenes, and I did again enjoy the challenge that Lynch presents with his difficult time lines and plots. I found it amusing that he included the Hollywood insider humor. At one point, I even considered that he shot on consumer DV to show his disdain for the Hollywood way, but I thought it detracted too much from the ideas he was presenting. Perhaps I just disagree with his approach to this video. The poor dialog and atrocious fake accents were almost unbearably annoying most of the time, as well. Wait a minute, this is one of Lynch's tricks, because you're actually watching a movie in a movie! Gotcha! If he was trying to strike up a discussion about how bad the movie was- on purpose- he succeeded.I will probably watch the movie again, just to check my thoughts, and I recommend that you watch it if you are considering. Don't make any judgments based on what I have said.</fullReview>

		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>David Lynch Punked Us</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The problem with Inland Empire isn't that it's hard to understand. In fact, unlike many of Lynch's other films, Inland Empire puts it all out there for us to see, up front. This is a disquieting thing for most Lynch-o-philes, who think "oh no-- there must be something more... I just have to wait for things to gel or reveal themselves..." Well, the problem is that it turns out Inland Empire is just David Lynch running Canadian hog-wild with a digital video camera. Seriously, who gave him that? That was a Bad Idea. OK, it was a mixed-results idea: Lynch has certainly shown us a thing or two about cinematography in DV, but at the same time DV has essentially ruined his film-making process by allowing him more freedom that he should have... essentially giving him more than enough rope to hang himself with.This all becomes abundantly clear at the veeeery end (think credits) of the film when, through a shorthand juxtaposition of characters and tropes you realize: "holy sh--, David Lynch is mocking me! He is laughing his head off at the viewer's expense!" Briefly, Inland Empire can be summarized as: David Lynch plays around in a new medium by creating a quintessentially Lynchian film universe that is utterly boring because there's nothing new in it- he's just re-hashing old ground. But just when you start to feel sorry for the old guy, thinking maybe this is the best he can do any more, here comes the last-minute reveal that shows you that he's just having fun at your expense. Because he can. Because he's David m-f Lynch.Really, Inland Empire should have a subtitle: David Lynch Punks Us.Disclaimer: I'm not sorry I saw this film. If I knew now what I knew then, and was presented with the option to make the choice again, I'd probably still see it. I probably won't, however, willingly see it a second or third time, like I usually do with Lynch's films.</fullReview>

Its not that the dialog is disturbing, its that the three people are having a conversation while something incredibly horrible is happening right before them and they accept it as an ordinary event happening on an ordinary night. In some ways we the audience accept it as inevitable as to what has been foreshadowed. It seems that this is going to be the last scene of the movie and in some ways it is but the story isn't quite finished. Lynch has more to say and creates the best sense of finality to any one of his movies or any other movie I have ever seen. 		<shortDescription>A movie about transmigrations of dharmas and fragmented self-experiences in the collective dream of maya that happens in the opening of the spirit</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>David Lynch avoided disclosing his own intentions (I assume it is a part of the school of filmmaking that is based on a viewer's response and avoids providing pre-given interpretations to viewers); but he did refer to the Upanishads at some points, saying: "We are like the spider. We weave our life and then move along in it. We are like the dreamer who dreams and then lives in the dream. This is true for the entire universe." Some people in the Internet found a translation from Mundaka Upanishad which says the similar thing: "As the spider creates the cobweb out of its saliva, it lives and plays in it and at the end the same spider swallows up the cobweb, similarly the God, the Lord creates the whole universe as the act of His thought. He manifests in it and again He withdraws the whole universe in Himself."I believe that INLAND EMPIRE is a riddle or koan which has keys to its resolution. I think those who intuitively embraced the keys managed to have their viewer experience transformed and allowed the film experience to transform them. It's definitely a difficult movie to watch and I saw people stopping watching it precisely at some points before the end of the second hour.
