		<shortDescription>Missing the point</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>David Lynch has quite the reputation, but with "Inland Empire" I think he is just missing the point. Indulging in one's whims might make for artistic freedom, but not necessarily for a good movie. And here Lynch discards film in favor of the hand-held digital camera, and also scripting in favor of semi-improvised takes. It doesn't work.His favorite technique of a storyline that loops around another storyline is repeated here for the third time (after "Lost Highway" and "Mulholland Dr"). But those were good films; clear, despite their complexity, and pure in their aesthetics. "Inland Empire" instead is a mess. Three hours of Laura Dern walking in endless corridors, and of close-ups of her face looking scared and puzzled. A monumental performance? Not really.The various story lines are unclear and unfocused, to the point that they do not warrant further investigation. Loss of identity, innocence, dreams within dreams, big bad Hollywood, yada yada yada. Yawn. If anything, what "Inland Empire" shows is the importance of the soundtrack. Various boring scenes are spiced up due to the usage of some amazing musical accompaniment. This would make for a nice video-clip then.Oh sure the art-house elite will love it. An auteur's piece, they'll say. What a shame that people are prepared to like such pretentious lazy nonsense just because it's a film by David Lynch. Coincidentally, Lynch has gone the businessman route lately, making his own line of coffee. Maybe you should focus on your future career, David?</fullReview>

I just realised that perhaps I may have been a bit harsh on Lynch and less so on the film. It is the film that is a catastrophe, not Lynch. I love Lynch's weirdness, but this is not one of those times. Watch the film at your own discretion. 		<username>xXBoss-EbertXx</username>
		<score>0.2</score>
		<shortDescription>Lame, Misguided, and a Putrid Smelling Mess</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Inland Empire is an mix of horror stories that have inexplicably garnered the praises of many fans of Japanese cinema, thus earning itself an 8/10 average rating on IMDb. Unfortunately, the film simply doesn't deserve the high rating, and it most definitely does not deserve 164 minutes of anyone's time.As a horror feature, Inland Empire fails miserably. Quite literally, there is not one scene in any of this dreadful backfire that is even marginally disturbing. In fact, the attempted horror was so badly executed that it came off as comedic. Oooh, I'm scared. If anyone is looking for a few chills, stay far, far away from this one.The lack of horror is not compensated in the least with interesting dialogue or dramatic elements. The acting is not very good, and almost no interesting narrative occurs in the films 164 minutes. When one tries to find some kind of point or moral to these stories, one finds that there is very little actual content to warrant an objective conclusion. The filmmakers seemed intent on creating the most superficial, meaningless plot lines and characters possible. The motives for the supernatural entities are usually ambiguous. In addition, Inland Empire was just plain boring. And no, I don't mean slow-paced; I mean boring. Old Yeller was slow-paced, but interesting. Forrest Gump was slow-paced, but interesting. I could list another 100 deliberately-paced films that move slower than molasses in January, but were still riveting. Inland Empire is just boring.There's almost nothing in this film that's noteworthy, but the exception to this is its cinematography. The special effects were used well to create an otherworldly atmosphere, this is just window-dressing to an otherwise shallow, below-average film that is in the end not very entertaining at all.What annoys me the most is when defenders of this film claim that it is far superior to Asian horror films made during the past decade. Such a claim is totally misguided. Inland Empire has NONE of the masterful storytelling found in A Tale of Two Sisters (2003), NONE of the impending doom-like atmosphere of Kairo (2001), NONE of the creepiness or cleverness of Ju-on: The Grudge 2 (2003 theatrical), NONE of the interesting concepts found in Cure (1997), and NONE of the psychological contributions seen in Audition (1999), just to name a few. Basically, Inland Empire has NOTHING memorable whatsoever, while the Asian horror films of the past decade have just about EVERYTHING except the convenient luxury of being made before 1970 - a time where every Japanese movie was a "work of art" or a "masterpiece." Give me a break.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A Cinematic Nightmare of Epic Proportions</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I seriously think David Lynch may have gone to bed one night, had the scariest dream in the history of mankind, and then decided to document it.Inland Empire is a very difficult movie to watch. I've had the DVD since August of '07 and it still smells brand new inside because I've opened it and watched it only twice.It is really hard to mentally prepare to view this movie, so more than a review, this is a suggestion on how to view the film.Inland Empire begins as oddly as any Lynch film ever made. A young woman, who's face is blurred out, is led into a hotel room by a man who's face is blurred out. It's black and white, and they exchange some odd dialog in polish about how they're about to have sex.After their conversation, the shot becomes completely distorted and she begins saying "Where am I? I'm afraid." The next shot is in color and it's a girl crying, naked and watching a television with no reception. Then we cut to a shot of three people in rabbit suits who are discussing a secret in incoherent dialog and a very creepy laugh track.That's the random nature of this film.Mulholland Dr. took multiple viewing before I finally got it. It's now one of my top 10 favorites of all time. Inland Empire is 10x more difficult to understand than Mulholland Dr., but there is something there. I just haven't completely figured it out yet.But I'm getting there.This isn't a fun movie to watch, but it is a challenge, and a riddle, and so it can be fun if you like the challenge and feeling of power of choosing whatever you want the movie to be.If you feel it's about nothing, than that's exactly what it's about. If you feel it's about a young woman trapped in her subconscious, that's what it's about, etc.Tips for viewing...* Have a clear head when you watch this * Watch this during the day, because night will contribute to the scary nature of this film and distract you from what's going on * Don't try and figure it out while the movie is going on, because you won't * Soak up as much info as you can, possibly on back-to-back viewings and then try and fit all the pieces together * Remember Lynch's love of illusion and that what you see may not always be real Just take all the info and at the end try and figure it out. Soak up everything. If you try and figure it out while it's going on, you'll keep getting thrown off. Just look at it like you're figuring out a riddle, and the info you need to get it is coming in pieces.I'll give this movie a 10 for it's intrigue, and because I like the plot (What I believe the plot to be, anyway. ;) )</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A Huge Disappointment.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>David Lynch is one of my favorite directors. I've enjoyed every film he has ever done. "Eraserhead", "Blue Velvet" and "Lost Highway" remain 3 of my favorite works of Lynch. After the excitement of "Muholland Drive", I was anxiously awaiting for "Inland Empire". I must say I felt let down. "Inland Empire" is the story of Nicki Grace (Or at least one character of many stories) played by Laura Dern. Nicki is an actress who just got a role in a big Hollywood production. Little to her knowledge does she realize the film is a remake and the 2 lead actors from the original film were killed due to a Polish Gypsy curse put on the production. The first hour of "Inland Empire" had me glued to the screen. It started out beautifully, but the last 2 were a complete disaster. It had such a great cast, but I felt Juia Ormond, William H. Macy and Jeromy Irons weren't in it enough. So many amazing actors that weren't used to their full potential. There roles felt too brief. I admit, Grace Zebriskie was hilarious as the crazy Polish neighbor. Too bad we didn't see more of her. I enjoyed Laura Dern's outstanding performance and I liked the sitcom with the Rabbit head people. But beyond that, there wasn't much else. Interesting note, the Rabbit sequence was taken from one of Mr. Lynch's short films from his website called "Rabbits". I love surrealist cinema, but similar themes have been done before and much better in films like Luis Bunuel's flawless dark comedy, "The Discreet Charm of the Bourgeoisie" (1972) and Shuji Terayama's forgotten masterpiece "Pastoral: To Die in the Country" (1974). More recently films like Andrzej Zulawski's "La Fidelite" (2000), and Sion Sono's disturbing "Strange Circus" (2005) remain unseen. Speaking of Polish films, Wojciech Has' multi-layered "The Hourglass Sanitorium" (1973) is an emotional puzzle worth figuring out. I feel these above films mentioned give more food for thought. Digital video usually looks pretty good, but "Inland Empire" is way to dark, sloppy and shaky. It looks like a bad experimental college film. Then again I think he was going for a raw look. I'd recommend the strange and somewhat similar low budget gems "Mad Cowgirl" (2006) or "The Three Trials" (2006) as great examples of what can be done with digital video. I love experimental films, but they either work or they don't. For example Oliver Stone's "Natural Born Killers" (1994) shows that a mainstream director can put together a visually stunning experimental work. The last 2 hours of "Inland Empire" feels like poorly shot random scenes thrown together with no coherent meaning. At least 3 short films were put into "Inland Empire" and it doesn't work. The rest of the movie was made up of things David Lynch wrote quickly before shooting. The outdoor barbecue scene and the prostitutes doing the locomotion were painful to watch. I still think Lynch had good intentions of trying something different, but fell short. If you want to see bizarre surrealist films that are not only visually stunning but with deep religious, philosophical and political meaning; check out the films of Alejendro Jodorowsky. Anchor Bay recently released Jodorowsky's films "El Topo" (1970) and "The Holy Mountain" (1973) in a box-set. I love you David, but please let this be a one time thing. Then again, my opinion could always change in the future?</fullReview>

		<username>MightyAlz</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Maybe Lynch's best, depending on your point of view...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>You could argue that Inland Empire shows a full circle completed. Lynch started his career with the abstract dreamscape of Eraserhead and since then until about the early 90's has gradually tuned down his vision into more commercially digestible form. Arguably the peak came with his masterpiece Twin Peaks and its incredible success with the viewers. Since then though, after the savage mauling of his criminally underrated Twin Peaks: Fire Walk With Me, he has been gradually been drawing the abstractions back into his work until now he seems to have gone back into Eraserhead territory. So is it any good?Well, it seems to me that the big question is - what the hell was that about? Well, I know the answer - nothing whatsoever. It doesn't make sense because it can't make sense - Lynch wrote the scripts on the day of filming and took numerous sources for the film from works completed years before. The Rabbits for example, were a short and rather creepy movie he placed on his website in 2002. Why he has integrated them into the film I don't know - but they work. And that's the thing; it may not make any sense, but it doesn't need to. When you look at a painting by Salvador Dali or Francis Bacon it doesn't make sense. Oh sure, there will always be people who will try and put meanings to the works, but that's always just an opinion - the paintings represent pure consciousness, a stream of thought in a reality which doesn't work like you think.In fact, that's the key to all of Lynch's films, especially IE - dream logic. You see, when you see most films with supposed "dream sequences" it never actually reflects a dream - they're always too logical, too coherent, too well put together. Lynch's films are not like this - they reflect the true dream logic, which is that you never know you are having a dream and everything makes sense to those inside the dream. IE fucntions to this logic throughout. It is pure thought.Right, now that the pretentious crap's over I'll get to the easier to digest facts about the film - firstly, it's the scariest film ever made. It's true that nothing in the world can scare you as much as a nightmare - it acts upon your basic instincts. Well, that's what IE does, more than even Eraserhead. Not to mention there's a scene towards the end which I won't give away that genuinely almost made me crap myself. I have never been given such a fright by any other film ever. I almost felt like I wanted the film to hurry up and end I was so freaked out.Another thing - the acting is absolutely fabulous by all concerned, but in particular Laura Dern - Lynch once said she was his favourite actress and you can see why. You'll never see a more disturbing or tragic performance given by any other actress for a long time.And of course it goes without saying that Lynch has all his trademarks in place - Red drapes, little dudes, flashing lights - but even more interesting is his use of Digital Video - some fans expressed complaints, but I have to say it makes the film all the more dream-like and it also gives him much more room for picture manipulation. It's perhaps the most beautiful film I have ever seen, if not in the traditional sense. One more thing - he makes a lot of references to his other films; remember the monkey from Twin Peaks:FWWM? Well...To conclude, if you're a Lynch fan and you haven;t seen this you need to be tazered - if you're not, well I wouldn't suggest making this your first film of his; it may throw you off a bit.Still, the best film of the millennium so far. Ya dig?</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>mikeymike111</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Brilliant yet deeply, deeply boring</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Inland Empire begins with a great scene - one of the best Lynch has directed. Film star Laura Dern receives a mysterious guest (Grace Zabriskie) who starts to ask questions about Dern's latest film. It's as funny as it is unsettling and Zabriskie gives a truly amazing performance. Occasionally she'll hold words back as if stopping herself from giving away too much information, or perhaps saying something very unpleasant. For the next half hour we seem to get the basic plot - the Dern film is a remake of a Polish original which has some strange history, is perhaps cursed. It seems an intellectual occult thriller like, for example, Foucault's Pendulum.But then the film becomes...well a word like 'fractured' undersells it. It's a collection of more or less random scenes which could unfold in any order and make as much - or as little - sense. There's a discredited theory which states that for a film to be successful you have to 'care' for the characters. Obviously you don't - but you do have to have some idea of their motivation, or the sense of what they're saying or how they feel about other characters. Even after two hours of the film Dern could walk into a room and you could have no idea who she is, where she is, why she's there or who the other people are (even though they, like Dern, have appeared before). One very much proved theory is that there is nothing more boring than someone recounting their dreams. "Well I was in a plane except it wasn't really a plane...it was my kitchen! And then I picked up an apple and my Mum came in and said I should put it in the fridge, so I did but I then realised etc etc etc." Inland Empire is three hours of someone recounting a meaningless dream and it's boring. It's excruciating. I lost the will to live and died literally twice during its running time.That's not to say the film doesn't have flashes of brilliance. No one does rooms like Lynch. He can make Deco furniture sinister, an emerald bedspread radiate pure evil. The rabbit sitcom room (oh, don't ask) is somehow like a vision of hell even though it's just...a room. And the vaguely industrial hum on most of the soundtrack is a great way of notching up the dread factor. But by the end I was thinking that if there was another shot of Dern running up some dark corridor to find something strange at its end (a roomful of whores, a fat-faced sweaty man, a cinema showing Inland Empire, a room with a man and a gun...the list is endless) I may start to cry.I think the film may come into its own when it's released on DVD. Lynch has, in effect, supplied the audience with a movie's raw footage which we can import into iMovie and then cut into our own version. Simple maths shows there are many billions of ways of assembling as few as 50 shots, and Inland Empire has a lot more than 50 shots. Some people will recut the whole 3 hours, others will give us a more manageable 90 mins. Someone will just give us the uncut rabbit sitcom and nothing else. If I could face watching it again even I'd want to maybe give it a go.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2007-03-13</date>
		<username>Red-Barracuda</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Baffling but unrewarding</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Being a huge David Lynch fan I was looking forward to this film enormously. After the majesty of Mulholland Drive what would Inland Empire be like? Well, the truth is I'm still not sure. However, it's only fair to acknowledge that I am somewhat disappointed.I'll start by admitting that I have no clue as to what this movie was about. Early on I lost the plot but figured I would roll with the punch and enjoy the ride. The lack of understanding wasn't something that I was so worried about. However, make no mistake, this is no Lost Highway or Mulholland Drive; films that do not make full sense on first viewing but which captivate the viewer nevertheless through their seductive execution. No, Inland Empire is an entirely different beast. It's one of the least commercial movies you are ever liable to see made by a name director. And while this in itself is a commendable thing, I could not help but find it all a somewhat frustrating viewing experience.The most obvious problem is the running time. 3 hours is very excessive for this film. I could not help but think this could've been edited down to a much more rewarding 90 minute movie. I think that the freedom that digital video has given Lynch has conversely compromised his film-making in that he has lost all discipline. A full-on no-holds-barred Lynchian work of art is, in theory, a great idea but it became increasingly obvious as this movie dragged on that he benefits far more from weaving his magic around a project with a tighter focus, the beautifully constructed dream logic of Mulholland Drive, for instance. Inland Empire, by comparison, is unfocused and unengaging. And to make matters worse, there really are very few moments of pure genius here that can be found in all his other work and that are a justification in themselves.On the plus side, this film does have a feeling all of its own. The atmosphere really works at times. He still uses music well as a direct accompaniment to events on screen. And this does lend itself well to the feeling of the movie. The acting is very detached - imagine Patricia Arquette and Bill Pullman's early exchanges in Lost Highway stretched out for 3 hours! ? but Laura Dern is very good in what must've been an extremely baffling role for an actor to tackle.And I liked the rabbits.This review is just an initial impression. I am unable to include very much analysis of meaning as I genuinely was perplexed on first viewing. I really, really wanted to like this but I just simply cannot ignore my reservations. Despite all this, I will definitely revisit Inland Empire on DVD. I am sure that there is more to it and am willing to reassess. I may even post a follow up review when that day comes. But for the moment I remain a little underwhelmed.</fullReview>

		<date>2007-01-02</date>
		<username>TonyDood</username>
		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>What This Movie Is Really About...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>To paraphrase a joke, what this movie is really about is...about 3 hours. The first thing anyone wants to know about "Inland Empire" it seems is, does it have a "meaning," a puzzle piece missing that, once you have it, makes the movie easier to understand?As far as understanding, you can't describe the "plot" of "Inland Empire." Other reviewers have given detailed accounts of what "happens," and that's about the best you can do with the material.What "happens" is that David Lynch turns on his camera and sets it in front of people and things, then cuts the images he recorded together in a way that pleases him. Self-indulgent movie-making is nothing new. Goddard was hailed by many as a genius for doing something similar with his films in the 60's and early 70's. So were Fellini and Bergman. Bunuel and Dali did it as far back as the silent era, Ken Russell and a host of others, including Woody Allen, have dabbled in this technique to this day, with varying degrees of success The truth is, it's very freeing to do "abstract" art, from the standpoint of the creator of the art. Whether the art created is something the public will enjoy is something a true abstract artist can't be concerned with...it's at complete odds with the concept of abstraction. If he "looks down" he might fall when he realizes that his art really only reflects that he was too lazy or untalented to create something more accessible.I'm glad Lynch created "Inland Empire." When Lynch is good he is very good indeed. There are many moments in "Empire" that resemble the "monster behind the restaurant" sequence in "Mullholland Drive"; small, isolated moments within a greater whole that are satisfying on their own and summon up the full spectrum of emotion. The film is frustrating, irritating, funny, moving, scary and sad all at the same time, and parts of it race directly for the deeper parts of your brain pan...and will stay there, whether you let them or not.The night I saw the film about 20% of the audience left around the two hour mark, and I don't blame them. It IS long and self indulgent...many of the shots look as if Lynch, rather than going to the bother of working hard on coming up with a solid, entertaining script, literally said to Laura Dern, "Let's go out back and shoot more of the movie...OK, in this scene you are (yet again) really scared, crying and screaming! Action!" Worse, he continually teases the audience with hints of a deeper meaning or a "real" story underneath the fever dream. But just when you settle down and get comfortable suddenly he cuts away, as if his Ritalin has suddenly worn off and he's lost interest in what he was doing. It's a challenge Lynch sets up. How long can you free fall without the safety net of traditional storytelling? "Empire" really does look a lot like someone's over-wrought film-school thesis...but isn't that what "Eraserhead" was? And that movie is now a classic. Time will tell about "Empire," of course.But is it any good? I admit I'm disappointed in the film visually. It was easy to forgive the obscure "story" of "Eraserhead" because that film was so fascinating visually (and aurally) it drew you back, and the more recent Lynch films are similar. "Empire" has several memorable moments in its epic running time, but nothing to rival the set pieces the director has become famous for. It's a small film and the rewards do come but only if you are extremely patient (the "Locomotion," an alarming "pop out" gag in a hallway near the film's end, Laura Dern on the streets of Hollywood, the end credits). Everyone will take something different away from the movie and Lynch probably would be happy with this; he set up the world and inhabited it...you make sense of it.And if that's what he wanted, if this film is truly meant to be explored and interpreted in any way the viewer chooses, like a painting by Magritte or Dali, it works fantastically. Like a dream, it can possibly be "explained" but only, truly, by the dreamer himself who actually FELT the dream. The rest of us have to content ourselves with interpreting what he shares with us to describe what he saw and how it felt; our interpretation can only be based on shared symbols we all agree upon. If, in fact, Lynch wants us to find a "story" in his movie, a beginning, middle and end that you have to work out like a puzzle...he's failed miserably, and the work has to be deemed amateurish, indulgent, ugly and slightly sad--the ultimate joke being that the movie is little more than a mental j.o.-fest that he's asking us to pay admission to see, because we so remember the joy of his midgets, log ladies, foul-mouthed pervert villains and Naomi Watts as a bitter lesbian. There have been many writers and filmmakers (and musicians) who are content to create for the sake of creation, without rules, and know that there IS meaning there, somehow, simply because the work exists. However, most of them are young and lack any sense of artistic discipline, they haven't "earned" the right to place a can of soup in the middle of a floor and call it art. In any other director's hands, "Empire" would probably be like a lot of disgruntled viewers are probably thinking it is...a meandering mess that goes on too long and waste's the viewer's time and money. However, I personally don't mind going for a ride with Lynch, even if there's no ultimate destination. Can you enjoy working on a puzzle that has no square edges, and some of the pieces actually missing? If you can, you'll enjoy this movie a lot.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2006-12-21</date>
		<username>MisterWhiplash</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Inland Empire- where the stars make dreams, and dreams make stars...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The bulk of that line refers to a quote from the film Inland Empire- spoken in a cameo by William H. Macy as a TV show announcer- and one might be tempted to use that as the template to try and 'figure out' what happens through the course of the film's three hours. It is indeed, especially on a second viewing, the most intriguing and useful theme for what goes on with the character of Nikki (or Sue, depending on the scene or mood of the moment), as she goes through what can be described as, well, indescribable. But the line is helpful to look at what director David Lynch is most after and interested by with the film- dream logic. More over, the dream logic of life, and how it relates to the cinema, and to perceptions, and to, as the tag-line suggests, "a woman in trouble." But all of this still can't really help me, even on a second viewing, give at all an idea of what to expect with Inland Empire. It's a dense, ambitious, graceful, crude, ambiguous, and flawed work of the highest order of abstractions put together into one big Lynchian package. And don't forget the rabbits with that.The first time I saw the film, I was more swept up into the emotional mix &amp; match that Lynch threw up on the screen, and by the immensity of how beautiful he was able to put everything together through his DV camera (like with Eraserhead, he plays with a form that is difficult in some ways, but inviting and exciting in others). The second time, I thought that it could be another excursion as Mulholland Drive was, where I could focus more on piecing whatever the hell kind of puzzle Lynch put up for the audience to possibly figure out. About half an hour into the film, however, I realized it was an act of futility to 'figure it out', at least in any conventional sense. But this is not necessarily a bad thing, even if it leaves the audience sharply divided as to take it: the greatest mind-bender of the new millennium, or the pretentious ramblings of a director un-hinged? I maybe went more with the former, even as I could understand the latter; it allows so much thought to come out of what happens in terms of characters' behaviors, of themes that emerge, and what might *seem* to come together when it really doesn't. What it lacks in narrative flow it makes up for with an originality in delving into personality, and what personality even means.At first it might seem as if there's a simple story here- Laura Dern as Nikki gets the part she's been waiting for, in a film by the great director Kingsley (Jeremy Irons), and with a fine co-star (Justin Theroux, even better here than in Mulholland). The actors find out though in a moment of confession from Kingsley that the project they're about to work on is technically a remake of an unfinished film where on the project the two main stars were killed, as there was a kind of 'curse' on the project called "47". It isn't long after this that Inland Empire gets even weirder than before, but by weird will be totally subjective to the viewer (and may depend as well on how much you're familiar with Lynch's world- or thought you knew what it could hold). Lynch then submerges Dern's character(s) into a landscape of suffering and bewilderment, where sub-plots possibly emerge involving a Polish circus, a murder via screwdriver, a "sitcom" TV show involving rabbits on a couch, and Nikki/Sue caught up in the maelstrom of an abusive downfall.A lot of this madness that comes through in Lynch's style, which is as audacious and remarkable and totally mesmerizing as it is going almost over the edge with its delirious need to push the dream logic and surrealism to its limits, could not be completely of worth without Laura Dern, who in particular is almost on equal footing of Lynch with heart and soul poured in. As required for looking at what it means to be an actress, or what it means to be a person, or to have a reality shattered by various un-realities, Dern is always perfect in relaying her emotions, however intense or drained or shocked or horrified they may be (or, sometimes too, of just listening to someone, like in that wonderfully batty scene shared with Grace Zabriskie as a 'neighbor'). It's probably the pinnacle of her career thus far, with everything she's ever either hinted at or shown as an actress up till now thrown up on the screen to match up with the wild demands of Lynch's own mind-set. It won't win an Oscar for her, but she should at least get a nomination, if it even means anything at this point. In a sense, this IS what acting is all about, at least for the sake of what Nikki has to do to play Sue, and vice versa, if that make sense.So on a second viewing did I understand Inland Empire any better than the first time? Probably not by much, and I actually still felt the flaws that pecked away the first time, namely that it does go too long, becoming sluggish towards the end (albeit with an astounding end credits sequence), and every so often a moment or scene felt too over the top. Yet overall, this is a film filled with so many ideas, so many visions, and so much to cast into one's mind about the nature of reality, of what it means to see an un-reality (either through the media, or through a form, or just in front of one's eyes like Dern with the "hot" girls who dance to the 'Locomotion'), and how things are never, ever as they seem. Just like Hollywood, one might say.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>So I just returned for David Lynch's Chicago premiere of INLAND EMPIRE at the Music Box. I should begin by stating that I'm a big DL fan. Now with that being said, I can honestly say that I found little redeeming qualities in INLAND EMPIRE. To begin with, the narrative goes absolutely nowhere. But OK, that's typical. What isn't typical is:1.Terrible cinematography-the single most distracting aspect present in IE. Knowing DL's background, I was expecting great lighting, compositions, and steady cam work. What I got was non stop shaky camera work, unflattering closeups, and dark grainy stairways. Now I am aware that this is shot digitally, but that isn't necessarily a problem. Look at '28 days later' or 'Looking for Kitty' to show how digital can be effectively achieved. Director of Photography Peter Deming is sorely missed here, and we now see how much he actually added to DL's pictures in the past. 2. Lack of music- Again, DL's stubbornness in doing everything himself hurts the film, this time with music. Angelo Baldamenti is nowhere to be found, and leaves DL to play around with Roland Synth pads to create ambiance. 3. Editor- I know I'm repeating myself here, but same as above, our Jack -of-all-trades DL is back at it, this time editing. His decision to leave basically 10 minute long diatribes sorely slows things down to audible snores being heard from the crowd.As you can see, I definitely think our friend DL has fallen from grace, and this film is one that you should have your head checked or at least seek therapy before undertaking. Also of note, David Lynch had a Q and A session after the film completed. I was so underwhelmed with what I saw that I headed for the isles while others stuck around for DL's tripe recipes.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Inland Empire explained! (MAJOR spoilers)</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Let me say, that I, Dan "The Movie Man" Wolfson have done it again. I have like in all of the previous Lynch films, managed to unearth the rich textural subtleties and secrets that lie within the film's tombs of confusion.It all lies in the name Axxon N. If you remember correctly, Axxon N. is the longest running radio program in all of Bulgaria (or somewhere strange and European) that is mentioned at the beginning of the film. Think about this. This is the first feature length movie that Lynch has shot on DV. I'm going to assume there will be more. Now, if that next one was Movie B this would clearly be Movie A. Add that A to Axxon N. Rearrange the letters.AXXON N. NO XANAX.The actress, played by Laura Dern in her best performance since Jurassic Park is obviously not on Xanax. She seems depressed. Confused. Wandering from one murky hallway to the next. As I watched the brilliant images flash before me one by one. I realized this Phantom character represented the darkness or depression in Laura Dern's life. When she shoots him at the end with the gun she pulls out of nowhere (the gun of Xanax, I should say. Xanax was developed by a Polish Doctor Of Medicine named Dr. Tryzcwk Gunn) he is vanquished and everyone is happy again. Light and life return to the picture. Everyone hugs each other! A family is reunited (I don't know what they were all about though). There's lots of bright lights and EVERYONE DANCES! The woman that Laura Dern kisses represents the pharmaceutical industry, which America has turned it's back on.It's no secret that Lynch is part of the crazy meditation cult. The Scientologists are their arch Nemesis's. I know L. Ron Hubbard would love for nothing more than to see the Maharishi Yogi lose his followers. Scientologists are against medication for depression, so obviously since these guys are like the Ying to each other's Yang -- the Yogi is totally for medication. I think it's great that they're out there fighting -- almost like the two Zoroastruthian Gods. Clearly, we know the battle lines that Lynch has drawn.If you are depressed like Nikki and keep thinking you're being stabbed in the stomach, get on Xanax. Take it from me, Dan "The Movie Man" Wolfson.(P.S. The girls who play the hookers are amazing actresses!)</fullReview>

		<username>TheLittleSongbird</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Very intriguing but didn't entirely work personally</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>As someone who appreciates David Lynch and loves most of his stuff, Inland Empire both captivated and frustrated me. It is not his worst film, that will always belong to Dune, but it is his least accessible film in the way that Blue Velvet, The Elephant Man and The Straight Story are his most, along with Mulholland Drive they are to me far among his best. It is very easy to see why a lot of people would and do dislike Inland Empire. The film starts off very promisingly but really drags a little over 2/3s of the way through to the extent that you do feel like it's an hour too long. The story is very jumpy structurally and many, including myself around halfway through will be as lost as Nikki herself trying to completely understand what's going on. And some of the script is even more of a convoluted jumble than Wild at Heart's, even the actors on occasions look bemused by it. The use of digital is a mixed bag, sometimes it's effective and fits with the atmosphere of the scene but there are other times where it looks amateurish and overused, the least polished Lynch film perhaps technically. That's not to say at all that the film looks bad because actually most of the visuals aside from the use of digital look great, especially in the bold colours and lighting that are both bewitching and creepy, while Lynch's direction is immediately recognisable and works particularly well in the most surreal moments. While Inland Empire is problematic for many narratively it actually works amazingly as a mood piece; there is some humour of the black humour kind-in distinctive Lynch style- and it does amuse at least and doesn't feel out of place, the surreal and even at times nightmarish atmosphere is often unflinchingly terrifying and in a way that is so captivating that you can't look away and there are some beautiful touches like with the lighter near the end. This atmosphere is enhanced by the soundtrack that is as positively entrancing as it is hauntingly beautiful, and Inland Empire has some great acting especially from Laura Dern whose astonishing lead performance marks as a career-best for her. Justin Theroux is very good and Jeremy Irons clearly looked as though he was having a ball. The support roles are acted more than dependably and nobody jars with the tone with the film. Overall, an intriguing that could have been great, it was easy to get drawn into the film but it was just as easy to get frustrated with it. Ranking it in his filmography, personally it'd be second weakest. 6/10 Bethany Cox</fullReview>

I mention the Kubrick film because at times the tone, the suggestion of time hopping and repeat characters, the grand designs of some of the rooms, the random shock moments and certainly the music.Blue Velvet and Lost Highway were true rock'n'roll movies, they had confidence, style, involved the viewer, and were far more emotional that IE because they had much more suggestive dynamics. E.g. Fred envying his wife in LH, Jeffrey discovering his own darkness in BV. Since Mulholland, Lynch has developed this too understated style where people purposefully repeat themselves too often, talk in monotone, everything is slow, and the film is too distracted by itself to allow a proper flow of feelings.Something for all the pro-reviewers to think about: if this film had been made by a first time student filmmaker, there is absolutely no way it would receive praise. I think people are influenced by the implication that being a David Lynch film, it simply must be good so everything is seen through that filter. I think that suggestible people will enjoy this film more, because they believe they should.In the end I found it very difficult to 'feel' this film. This isn't to say I didn't 'get it' as I find that a snobby and pretentious argument. It is more that the film doesn't want involvement, the characters and situations beg for awkward self-consciousness that draws attention to itself and alientates any potential emotions. When Laura Dern is talking like a whore, I wasn't 'feeling' her emotion, I was thinking 'this doesn't seem at all credible, her swearing seems forced and contrived, the guy she is talking to looks like hes from a sketchshow, why should I invest my time in this self-conscious attention seeking'. Overall I think very overrated - for three hours of investment I expected so much more.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>David Lynch down the rabbit hole...if actions really do have consequences, what is the penance for complete self-absorption?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>David Lynch carves out another skittering, schizophrenic, skin-crawling mental-mystery with "Inland Empire", which has recognizable elements lifted from other David Lynch films...the most familiar of which is an overall obtuseness which makes the picture difficult to grasp in one sitting. Hollywood actress Laura Dern learns the new picture she's working on is the second attempt to film the story, an adaptation of a Polish legend; the first version resulted in a shut-down after the two leading actors were murdered and the material was said to be cursed. Dern's wealthy, powerful husband uses strong-arm tactics to remind his wife's leading man that their marriage vows are tantamount, while Dern's personality splinters off into different incarnations that seem to have one thing in common: the threat (or fear) of murder. Without reading other comments or professional movie critiques, I am not quite sure why the Polish man's teary wife appears to be the spark plug for the initial set-up, watching a rabbit-headed play on a television screen, or why the Loco-Motion prostitutes don't acknowledge Dern while she's in their presence. I can figure why one of the hookers lifts her T-shirt and shows us her breasts. Lynch has a deep-rooted fixation with trailer-trash life, and paradoxically doesn't think much of low-class women; using them as pawns, or as objects to be ogled, he reasserts his heterosexuality by not showing remorse or scorn for their lives, but by showing off their bodies. I think it's time Lynch got his movies out from the Hollywood milieu, away from the street whores, and streamlined his narrative for more inclusion. I don't care that his dialogue is so elliptical it stretches the picture out to a three-hour length (the pacing is nimble enough that you hardly notice the time), but by being so emotionally (and dramatically) imperceptive, I get the feeling Lynch is laughing at the audience--not with them. ** from ****</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Empire of the Senseless</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There are really two people who need to be praised for this film: Grace Zabriskie and the incomparable Laura Dern. Both of their performances are top notch and Laura Dern's is particularly astounding in its breadth and scope. That being said, I would hate for those great women to be hurt by my following review. What I have liked about Lynch films in the past is how beautiful they are, even if they don't make a lot of sense. The symbolism, imagery, cinematography all and sheer emotion make up for a sometimes atypical narrative. I'm afraid this film lacks most of those positive qualities. Don't get me wrong, I am not upset that I can't figure the plot line out, or that this film is long. I just found it weaker than Lynch's other offerings. First of all, there was no need for the 3 hours, having just said I didn't mind the length. I liked that it took its time to start to unravel. This was most effective. But the dream-like sequences and "shocks" were less pointed and planned than usual. They were... well... just weak. Usually you leave a Lynch film feeling that you don't know what you saw, but you were enriched for having seen something you couldn't have possibly imagined yourself. Here we have too much emphasis on the fact that you are supposed to try to figure out what's going on. And, I have to say, that I think Lynch is suffering from an old, white, heterosexual stereotype. He is obsessed with prostitutes. And, I'm sorry, that's boring. The laughing, buxom, grotesque prostitute shots are abundant and overwrought. The only thing I couldn't get enough of was Laura Dern's talent. She is so amazing and gets to do so much in this film it's such a treat. But Lynch's contribution seems meager in comparison. Only Grace Zabriskie's extreme and menacing close-up at the very beginning of the film brought me unending joy. All of Lynch's other usual tricks, long hallways, bright flashes of light, spotlights in the face, ponderous, long, senseless dialog were heavy-handed. "Like I was in a dark movie-theater waiting for the lights to come up" is a line in the middle of the film, that is then replayed IN a movie theater later on. Come on, that's heavy-handed. And not the beautiful heavy-handed, either, it's mundane. I wish Lynch had a better self-editor on this one. It could have been great if it had been strung together at a better pace. See it if only for Laura Dern's power-punch of a performance. Oh, and leave before the ridiculous, mood-destroying final credits. Nina Simone has never been more misused.</fullReview>

		<date>2007-08-16</date>
		<username>Jonny_Numb</username>
		<score>0.2</score>
		<shortDescription>The Lynch of Diminishing Returns</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"I want to report a fraud," the drunken Beverly Mantle (Jeremy Irons) announces at a ceremony where his identical twin, Elliott, is accepting an award. "He's Beverly and I'm Elliott!" And this reference to David Cronenberg's seminal gynecological nightmare, "Dead Ringers," could very well sum up "Inland Empire," David Lynch's latest opus of switched-identity incoherence, where-nothing-is-as-it-seems. The "fraud" comes in when you discover that Lynch has come up WAY short in giving us a narrative worth investing in, or characters worth caring about. More often than not, this ugly-looking, overlong slug of a film plays like a practical joke on those who have spent long hours dissecting the buried plots of "Eraserhead," "Lost Highway," and "Mulholland Drive." Yes, now the long-term Lynch detractors can point their fingers and say, "I told you so!" While my mind is still revisiting certain images invoked during the 3-hour "Inland Empire," it is in a very scattered way?the images may be striking, yes, but do they really MEAN anything? And does the movie really engage us well enough to revisit multiple times to find out? Not really. Lynch, who has touted digital film-making in interviews, seems more like a kid showing off a new toy, and badly?the film is awash in murky hues, shaky framing, and an over-reliance on extreme close-ups that made me wish I'd taken Dramamine before viewing. The sound quality is maddeningly uneven?on the DVD, you'll be turning up the volume to hear the hushed dialog, only to be knocked out by a random loud sound effect (or maybe a bunch of hookers "doing the locomotion" as seizure-lights flash across the image), while our cinematic ringmaster laughs delightedly. But "Inland Empire" comes to a point where the dialog ceases to have much relevance...along with everything else.Last year, right around the time of "Inland Empire"'s theatrical release, I read an article that gave a revealing detail in that the film was shot according to ideas the director would write down and tell his actors to perform. This goes quite a ways in explaining how scattered the finished product feels (yes, even for a Lynch film)?our perpetually spaced-out auteur could have easily filmed "Inland Empire" over the course of a lone weekend with no regard for structure, and simply thrown the damn thing into theaters as is. Even in his most seemingly nonlinear films, there is a salvageable plot that warrants analysis and brings into focus even the most disparate elements. "Eraserhead," on the surface, is about a man's reluctant, awkward (and failed) adjustment to fatherhood; "Mulholland Drive" is a love story set against a Hollywood backdrop; and "Lost Highway" is a tale of lust and betrayal with Noir underpinnings."Inland Empire" bears the most in common with "Mulholland Drive"?at times, it seems like a very poor remake or sequel?but has no emotional or metaphysical anchor to keep us firmly attached to the story. It begins with Laura Dern (Lynch's "Blue Velvet") and Justin Theroux ("Mulholland Drive"), working on a film directed by Jeremy Irons; Harry Dean Stanton has an odd little supporting role as Irons' assistant. And things venture far into left field from there, as any pretense at a plot is abandoned, even the details that may hint at where we could FIND one? the more "Inland Empire" goes on, the less resonance anything has, and the more you're just waiting for the damn thing to end. Invasive weirdness set against familiar facades has always been Lynch's stock and trade (and is usually an asset to his films), but that we care nothing for Laura Dern's character, who is introduced hastily and undergoes all variety of hell before the film sputters to a conclusion, is a bad sign. Spending most of the film looking directly at the camera, as slack-jawed as the viewer, Dern had me longing for the more lively ladies (and story) of "Mulholland Drive." That being said, Dern can be only held partially responsible for the performance (though she would have been wise to outright turn the movie down)?again, it's Lynch's slack "script" and direction that leaves her adrift in nonsense wearing a mask of depth.At 3 hours, "Inland Empire" is a ponderous headache whose replay value will depend on individual viewer gullibility. Lynch has long been an advocate for artistic freedom and liberation from the confining studio system, but if all he has to offer us is dreck like "Inland Empire," then I'd say we'd be better off with his "mainstream" efforts.My rating of "2" is indicative of the fact that, at some point, I probably will revisit "Inland Empire"?many of Lynch's films are more revealing upon subsequent viewings, but as I give most films I comment on a single view before passing along my judgment, I believe "Inland Empire" should be subjected to the same treatment. If, in the future, it plays any better, I will be sure to amend this review.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Rubbish</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The people who gush over this film are simply not to be taken seriously.Firstly, it's shot with hand-held digital cameras (not the kind of digital used in, say, the recent Superman, where digital comes close to being a passable substitute for film if you lower you standards only a hair's breadth; rather, it's the kind of lousy, cruddy, mud-o-vision digital which makes you wonder why you bothered walking into the theatre) - with an absurd overuse of fish-eye lenses. And it's not even in focus all the time. I know the out-of-focus shooting was deliberate - well, actually I don't know, but let's suppose it was - but it adds insult to injury: the image definition isn't anywhere near good enough for even a half second's out-of-focus shooting to be endurable.Why do we stand for this kind of thing? Why don't people who pay good money to see a movie rise up in spontaneous rebellion when they find they've been swindled? It's one of those mysteries of modern life, like the mystery of why all modern buildings are ugly, when we certainly don't have fewer technical resources than our ancestors did when they made buildings that were beautiful.And secondly, Lynch's latest is about (inasmuch as it's about anything) an actress, and about the production of a film. It's set in Hollywood and most characters are film-makers of one description or another. Does the resulting work consist of tedious, puffed-up, self-referential tomfoolery? Is there any serious doubt? Mulholland Drive was a more lifeless, inferior knock-off of Lost Highway chiefly because it was about Hollywood rather than something external to Hollywood: the most versatile directors in the world have difficulty making films about films without turning out something tiresome and trite, and Lynch, although he has on occasion been a great film-maker, and although he did make The Straight Story, is not renowned for his versatility.One of the basic plot points is silly. Being based on a Polish folktale does not curse a production. Being set in Hollywood, does.Lynch usually relies heavily on his is-it-real-or-is-it-a-dream ambiguity, but in this instance, there's nothing else - and the lousy digital images mean that even this stalwart gimmick doesn't stand a chance of working. Obviously, in excrement-brown digital, it's all a dream. Or might as well be. The digital cameras have robbed Lynch of his power to invest mundane items with menace (in digital, the mundane simply looks even more mundane); they've preventing him from using his slow, gliding camera; they've led him into the by now well-known just-let-the-camera-operator-follow-the-actors trap, and encouraged him to be self indulgently profligate in compiling more and still more footage for the thinnest script he has ever worked on.You don't have to take my word for any of this, or trust my aesthetic subjective sense. Let's suppose for the sake of argument I'm a lousy judge of cinema. Nonetheless, the movie was shot on low-definition, muddy digital video: that's a known fact: look it up. The movie is set entirely in Hollywood and features actors, directors and technicians; again a known fact: look it up. You now know all you need to know to infer that Lynch has laid an egg.</fullReview>

		<date>2007-03-06</date>
		<username>jamesdalrymple2002</username>
		<score>0.2</score>
		<shortDescription>A mess</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There will always be a hardcore of Lynch fans who will love Lynch's special brand of weirdness for its own sake or otherwise, but I think this film is his weakest to date. Since Lost Highway his films have become more and more obfuscated and fragmented, paying little mind to coherence of plot or character. Whereas Mulholland Drive and Lost Highway amounted to highly atmospheric and sometimes intensely emotional experiences, Inland Empire feels too deconstructed to sustain interest. All the trademark atmospherics are there, but there is little in terms of character or narrative push to make the audience care. Apparently Lynch wrote every scene before shooting, and with its lo-fi digital technique, it feels like it has been made up as it goes along. Furthermore, at almost three hours, it feels amateurish and pretentious, like a terrible student film extended way beyond necessity. I think that no matter how abstract a film is, the audience needs a reason to care (especially at this length) and Lynch just doesn't deliver one on this occasion. Also, the visual and acoustic techniques that underpin the mood of this film seem largely regurgitated here, and as dislocated and nightmarish as it sometimes is, it never feels authentic. The sense that Lynch has run out of tricks a little is impounded by the cheap quality of some of the production. In one dislocated scene Laura Dern's character finds herself in some sort of brothel, and the lighting effect was obviously generated by shining torches in her face in a manner that recalls the acid trip sequence in Easy Rider. It's kitchen sink standard at times, but Lynch never punches above the weight of the apparent economic restraints. Overall it is a tedious mess.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>thehellhole</username>
		<score>0.3</score>
		<shortDescription>Fallen Empire</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>First of all, I'd like to say that I found David Lynch's Inland Empire to be a disappointment, not entirely, because there were a handful of good scenes, but overall it's a great mess, sugarcoated with bloated non-linear structure, which is so complicated that you start losing interest and simply don't care anymore. Personally I could not feel for the characters, because the film failed to touch me emotionally. It was just the opposite to all the other Lynch movies that I adore with great enthusiasm, especially Blue Velvet, Elephantman, Twin Peaks Series and even Eraserhead, that is a truly great work of abstract art. I like non-linear structure, because it makes you think but this one went too far, straight into self-parody.I watched the movie a few days ago, gave it time to rest in my mind and tried to figure out why I disliked it. It was not the DV-cinematography (it's a new technology with new possibilities), the actors were all great (well, most of them), the music and sound effects were really good, too. It was THE FORMULA that bothered me. David Lynch added nothing new to his world, except the DV technology. In a certain way, it was painting by numbers by David Lynch:"Mistreated Woman + Losing Reality + Immoral Counterparts (Men) + Threatening figure in the background (Husband) + Non-linear time structure + Spooky Sound Effects + Director Trademarks (Hallways, Curtains, Light Effects, etc.) + Beautiful singing voice at the end (Crysta Bell and DL on the synth) = David LYNCH MOVIE (TM)"Now, this formula was too obvious and leads automatically to disappointment, if you've seen all David Lynch movies and know his biography. You can compare and you can tell the differences. It does not have to do with form, it has to do with substance and whether an artist has to say something important or not. Maybe David Lynch said all there is and he uses Digital Video as a toy to experiment with new technologies. And Inland Empire just looks like that. But that's cool with me. The only problem is that I invested 3 hours of my life, spent 8 Euros and waited more than eight months to see his new work. The most positive thing I can say about Inland Empire is that it shows the possibilities of Digital Video. It inspires you to do a movie on your own. The quality is gritty and cold and very realistic (that's why DV works for porn and Reality TV so well) but with artistic lighting, a creative set-design and good actors you can create a masterpiece if you're lucky.The first hour of Inland is quite good, "the story" begins to unfold and there is a certain amount of suspense. There are a few brilliantly executed scenes, like the title sequence, the Marilyn Levins scene or one of my favorite, when Nikki/Sue realizes that she fell in love with Devon/Billy. You can see it in her eyes! Now that's great acting! Later on there is a death scene that is very disturbing but great and memorable, but the rest is not very intriguing. The end sequence is nice and ironic but I thought it was confusing and broke the mood. As a standalone it would be awesome. The rest of the movie is - like another reviewer wrote - overdosed mental masturbation. Nothing else.So, can I recommend this movie? The Lynch-Fans, like myself, who can't wait for it, should see it. For all the other people out there who are just curious, I suggest a DVD-rental.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>moimoichan6 (moimoichan6@yahoo.fr)</username>
		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Davis Lynch's got a new DV camera</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>It's quite hard to judge this movie with traditional aesthetic codes, for, if you immediately recognize the typical Lynch's themes and figures, it's such an experimental recherches laboratory, that you leave the theater without any judgment in your mind. It's nor good nor bad, it's fascinating and boring at the same time, unimaginable and yet predictable when you know Lynch's other movies. It's not a movie in the traditional sens of the term, but there's much more cinema in this movie than in most films released in theater."Mulholland Dr." was the narrative apogee of the interrogations Lynch asked years ago with "Blue Velvet", and formed, along with "Lost Highway", a coherent trilogy about cinema and the unreality of its world, witch drives its characters in the edge of madness. "INLAND EMPIRE" continues the formal experiences of the previous movies, but without their narrative coherence : it's like the themes of "Mulholand Dr." were mixes with the experimental style of "Eraserhead". The result is quite intriguing, but also a little disappointing : it's like Lynch, after what may be his absolute masterpiece, gives us his preparation works, instead of a finished piece of art. Of course, some scenes are beautiful (specialy at the end of the movie, for those who survive more than two hours) and some cinema ideas are really crazy, but the impression of incoherence still remains. It's seems the movie was think like a gigantic "work in progress" more than a classic narrative movie, but this lack of an identifiable storyline, whereas the movie brings you some narrative lines, rapidly creates a feeling of incoherence, and makes it looks like the artistic necessity is missing. For instance, the movie could have last one more hour (thank good it's not), or could be cut in half without interfering with its integrity. The others Lynch's movies didn't give this impression of arbitrary, but their complexity increased the narrative necessity of each scenes : you can't cut or add anything to "Mulholland Dr." or "Lost Highway" without breaking their unity : it doesn't seem true for "INLAND EMPIRE", where the logical cohesion of the movie seems much more blurry. It's even hard to say if the all movie isn't a big joke, and that Lynch gives incoherent frames without meaning, for an elitist audience who's only waiting this kind of meaningless montage from Lynch. Don't get me wrong here : Lynch is certainly one of the most brilliant director alive, as "Wild at heart" or "Twin Peaks" prove it, but you may doubt his sincerity when you watch "INLAND EMPIRE" and ask yourself if he's not just trying to mess with his audience... But even if my feelings are right (only time and other visions of the movie will tell), "INLAND EMPIRE", between two boring moments, hides a fistful of beautiful frames and magical ideas, and stays, no matter what, a hell of an experience.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Id Empire</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A strange, threatening, long and yet consistent film. The basic plot - rather like the actor/character crossover metaphysic of Mulholland Drive - centres around an actress not only in a cursed role but also with an out-of-synch consciousness. Not for nothing is the film within the film entitled On High in Blue Tomorrows.The grand joy of the film is it's texture. Shot almost entirely on digital film, it often has the look of a string of bizarre You Tube clips played in succession. The CCTV-ish grain of the texture really lends itself to Lynch's fine-line narration veering between realism and the surreal. Additionally, the editing is quite outstanding and the film maintains a coherent pulse.This is just as well because most of the time the substance of the film is either elusive or simply impenetrable. There is very little in dialogue or scraps of linear plot to guide one through the grand arc of the film (such as there is one). It doesn't help that there are one or two sequences that really tax the patience of the viewer.Lorna Dern's heroic performing stamina is a pretty good reason to commit yourself to three hours of this baffling but undeniably affecting opera. 4/10</fullReview>

Three reasons why this is not a good movie. 1. It's far too long. I can't think of a single reason why this film should go on for almost three hours, since half of the time you're watching completely meaningless or utterly incomprehensible scenes which don't help the story a millimeter forward. During the performance I attended, several people left the cinema. Others were clearly glad it was finally over. 2. Too much form, too little substance. An overdose of creepy dark hallways, disturbing close-ups and cheap horror effects would be forgivable if it all served a purpose. It does not. Of course somewhere deep beneath all the Lynchianisms that seem to go on forever, there is a story. Or two. Or three (I lost count). Intertwined of course, with references forward and backwards in time and space. But they're hard to find. 3. Laura Dern. She is a fine actress with some noteworthy performances in this film. But too much is too much. There's hardly a minute without her. And since there are 170 minutes in this film, that's a lot of Laura.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Confusion on too many levels to count</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Alright, to respond to people saying it reminds them of Lynch's early short films; this film may resemble that, but it's a brutal 195 minutes of madness, and thats pretty hard to take for anyone who wants a storyline with coherency and, er, plot.You can call this a SPOILER if you want; if you want to go in not having any idea what to expect, don't read: Like Muholland Drive, this film tries to bring you into one storyline and then pull the carpet out from under you to reveal another. However, this is done SEVERAL times. We're first brought into the storyline of Laura Dern's character, an actress who just landed a big part in a film. Lynch holds our hand through it, and here is where he shows his brilliance as a director. He sets up the atmosphere and the characters' dynamics with each other well. Then BAM, we're wandering through tangents with characters speaking in Polish and Laura Dern's own psychotic adventures... for HOURS.END SPOILERS After the first hour, I the plot fell apart and I was left for (at least) the next hour and a half unable to comprehend any of what was being told; the warping story lines and the confusing character relationships lost me. I was eventually brought back in to the story... only to soon be kicked out again.I will commend it for this though; near the end, there is one sequence where I saw what I deem to be the scariest image I have ever seen in a film. Right after it was shown, literally every audience member in the theater was giving a friend a look of "holy sh*t".As a film, Lynchs "Inland Empire" is crap. There's no single coherent storyline, there's hours of confusion and its length left me supremely bored. The confusion left me no reason to care for any of the characters, and I was left wondering just when and how the hell they were going to wrap it up. I would recommend it for those who want to explore a film that may be the equivalent of someone losing their sanity; not the hollywoodized, Donnie Darko kind of enjoyable sanity lost. Literally an exploration into what losing one's sanity would feel like. As a movie though... I can't really recommend it. Also, its filmed on DV cameras, for anyone that cares.</fullReview>

This is Lynch's most elusive tone poem to date, and its cheapo visual aesthetic links it to no other film in his canon What we get is an unsurprising uncomfortable 3 hours of unfathonable iterations .. Psychopathic sexual jealousy; rabbit sit coms, unwanted children; unreliable electrical currents, leading to exploding light-bulb effects and cavernous hallways --all Lynchian trademarks The same goes for Lynch's iterations of female innocence contrasted with female degradation. The director indicts the voyeur and is the voyeur. By the time the blond Dern releases the raven-haired Polish "lost girl" with a kiss, we're back, fleetingly, to the identity schisms informing "Blue Velvet" and "Lost Highway" and "Mulholland." (You know: the self-confident woman &amp; the jealous, possessive man &amp; the dreams of an actress/artist), but he even uses the same interiors as he does in Twin Peaks (the eighties rooms). I think it's daring to make your film without a script, but it has brought Lynch only to recyclage of the things he used to make. We may not demand a lot of David Lynch, since he is a master with the camera: he makes his own demands. But is an original story too much to ask? There may be a lot of critics and movie-fans who are going to juggle with expensive words as oniric odyssey, osmosis, postmodernist etc. and they are going to claim: "It wasn't entirely the same as Mulholland Drive or Lost Highway" but honestly: tell me you didn't look at your watch. Tell me you didn't look at your watch. No matter how complicated or simple you want a film to be, there's always going to be one critic who can find a complex narrative in it. And I have the impression that what Lynch is all about. Tell the world how deep &amp; insightful you are by watching and liking David Lynch-films. So David Lynch is actually a gift to the world and the people who are "expressive". On a more positive note, I thought there were some funny scenes too, especially with the girls-club (doing the Locomotion). But that was rather rare and it was more funny like when you see a dog with a funny tail: funny but not really. For the people who are already planning to go see it twice or three times: I respect that a lot. It shows you have great character. (The soundtrack was fantastic by the way: really nice of him at the end to start Sinnerman at the instrumental solo. Made my heart skip a beat.)</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Insanely Bad, Intensely Boring</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Dear David LynchI went to see 'INLAND EMPIRE' last night. Usually a three hour film has a three hour storyline, whereas yours had maybe 20 minutes of story in total. What on earth were you thinking? You've made a truly bad derivative student art movie. Lots of (and I mean LOTS) of shots of Laura Dern walking down corridors with a worried/scared/horrified look on her face, and endless supposedly 'meaningful' conversations, do not make tension, interest or even a decent cinematic narrative.I 'get' that supposedly the central reality is that its 'the thoughts of a woman in trouble' - in this case some Polish hooker. And yes, there's that arbitrary side plot of two people filming a Hollywood movie, and the ones' husband is jealous, and maybe there's a murder. And yes, the time duality thing is interesting. I don't need a 'linear' storytelling form in order to appreciate quality cinema - however, I DO need something more than what smells like a burned out artists' laundry list, disguised as a deep artistic creation.People began leaving the cinema eventually, as the hours trudged by, and endless scenes signifying nothing, kept appearing, which just kept completely defusing any dramatic tension you might have established initially. (Unless you meant to establish boredom by hours of 'meaningful' and 'portentous' dialog).David - I don't know if you've been surrounded by too many people who have been telling you that your every idle thought and foible is of great value, and thus causing you to lose what every artist needs - namely - a good objective perspective on your own creative output, but because I love you and your previous work, I'm taking the time to succinctly and sadly fulfill this critical function that seems to have failed you when you perhaps needed it the most.Listen to me David, I'm not willing to delude myself and try to pretend that what I saw was somehow meaningful or even 'good'. I don't care that you are boring your audience, I do care that you are deluding yourself perhaps, into having made a badly conceived piece of work, and are lowering artistic standards by attempting to make your audience have to pretend to themselves that their justified boredom was somehow 'good' or 'not there'. You are better than what you have created here.The public should be warned - its NOT 'Wild At Heart' 'Eraserhead' 'Blue Velvet' or 'Mulholland Drive' - each of which were truly great stories and even better Cinema. I would suggest that the public should not be fooled by any PR, or any prior goodwill that they justifiably have towards David Lynch, given his cinematic pedigree.'INLAND EMPIRE' is 'disturbing' - not because of its unique vision or storyline or narrative style - but because its so incredibly badly made, so utterly pretentious, narratively boring and incoherent, visually plodding, banal and badly edited, by a director who used to be so damn good.</fullReview>
